ثم لا يخفى ان اعتراضه على التعريف الثانى الخ غرض شارح تفتازانی اعتراضین علی المصنف - اعتراض اول یہ کہ مصنف نے تعریف ثانی ( جو کہ شوافع سے منقول ہے) پر ایک اعتراض وارد کیا ہے وہی اعتراض بعینہ تعریف اول پر بھی وارد ہوسکتا ہے یہاں مصنف نے سکوت کیوں اختیار کیا ہے اس اعتراض کو کیوں ذکر نہیں کیا وہ اعتراض یہ ہے کہ ھو العلم بالا حکام میں احکام سے کیا مراد ہے یا کل احکام مراد ہونگے یہ باطل ہے کیونکہ مکمل احکام غیر متناہی ہیں ان کا علم محال ہے یا بعض معین مراد ہونگے یہ بھی باطل ہے کیونکہ کل احکام غیر متناہی ہونے کی وجہ سے مجہول ہیں تو بعض کی تعین نہیں ہو سکے گی یا غیر معین مراد ہونگے وہ بھی مجہول ہیں لہذا یہ تعریف درست نہیں ہے وہی اشکال یہان معین مراد ہیں یا غیر معین وہی اشکالات یہاں بھی وارد ہو نگے جو تعریف ثانی میں وارد ہوئے تھے۔ جواب ! (1) تسلیم ہے کہ یہاں بھی یہ اشکال وارد ہو گا لیکن مصنف نے ادبا یہ اشکال ذکر نہیں کیالان هذ التعريف منقول عن الامام ابی حنیفہ ۔ جواب ! (۲) ثم اعلم سے مصنف نے فقط تعریف ثانی پر اشکال نہیں کیا بلکہ دونوں تعریفوں پر اشکال کرنا چاہتا ہے سعد نے یہ سمجھا کہ اعتراض فقط ثانی تعریف کے ساتھ خاص ہے اس لیے مصنف پر اشکال وارد کر دیا۔ جواب ! (۳) در اصل تعریف ثانی یہ ہے، جو العلم بالا حکام اور احکام سے کل احکام مراد نہیں ہو سکتے کیونکہ یہ غیر متناہی ہیں انسان کی طاقت سے ان کا احاطہ ناممکن ہے لیکن تعریف اول میں معرفتہ النفس مالھا ہے اور یہاں جمیع مالھا و ما علیھا مراد ہو سکتے ہیں کیونکہ مالھا و ما علیھا سے مطلق مالها وما عليها مراد نہیں بلکہ وہ مالھا وما علیھا مراد ہیں جو متعلق بالنفس ہیں اور نفس کو جو اپنی حیوۃ دینو یہ میں مالھا و ما علیھا پیش آتے ہیں وہ ان جمیع کی معرفت حاصل کر سکتا ہے لہذا تعریف ثانی والا اشکال تعریف اول پر وارد نه ہوگا ۔ مع ان اطلاق لفظ اعتراض دوم! مصنف نے فرمایا کہ مالھا و اعلیھا میں پانچ احتمالات ہیں تو اشکال یہ ہے کہ تعریفات میں ایسے الفاظ کا ذکر کرنا جو متعدد معانی کا احتمال رکھتے ہوں قبیح اور غیر مستحسن ہے جب کہ کسی معنی کی تعیین پر کوئی قرینہ نہ ہو یہاں بھی ایسا ہی ہے لہذا یہ تعریف غیر مستحسن ہے۔ جواب! (۱) قدماء کے نزدیک یہ غیر مستحسن نہیں ہے اور یہ تعریف بھی قدماء کی ہے۔ جواب ! (۲) لفظ محتمل للمعانی المتعد وہ کا استعمال في التعریفات اس وقت قبیح اور غیر مستحسن ہے جب کہ ان معانی میں سے فقط ایک ہی مراد وعلی العین لیکن اس معنی معین پر کوئی قرینہ نہ ہو اس صورت میں قباحت لازم آئے گی کیونکہ جہالت فی ا تعریف لازم آئے گی اگر لفظ محتمل للمعانی المحمد وہ ہو اور جمیع معانی مراد ہو سکیں تو ایسے لفظ کو تعریفات میں لانا نبی نہیں ہے یہاں بھی احتمالات خمسہ میں سے دو کو چھوڑ کر کیونکہ وہ جامع جميع الاقسام نہیں باقی تینوں مراد ہو سکتے ہیں یعنی مخاطب کو اختیار ہے جو مراد لے لے اس لیے تعریف میں کوئی قباحت نہیں ہے۔
Then it is not hidden that his objection to the second definition... The purpose of Sharh Taftazani is [to mention] two objections against the author. First objection: The author made an objection to the second definition (which is transmitted from the Shafi'is). That same objection could also be made to the first definition. Why did the author remain silent here? Why did he not mention this objection? That objection is: In 'knowledge of rulings', what is meant by 'rulings'? Either all rulings are meant – this is false because complete rulings are infinite, knowledge of them is impossible – or some specific rulings are meant – this is also false because all rulings, being infinite, are unknown, so specific ones cannot be determined – or unspecified rulings are meant – they are also unknown. Therefore this definition is not valid. The same problem arises here whether specific or unspecified [is meant]. The same objections that were made to the second definition also apply here. Answer (1): We concede that this objection also applies here, but the author, out of respect, did not mention this objection because this definition is transmitted from Imam Abu Hanifa. Answer (2): Through 'then know that' the author did not limit the objection to the second definition; rather he intended to object to both definitions. Sa'd thought the objection was specific to the second definition, so he made an objection against the author. Answer (3): In reality, the second definition is 'knowledge of rulings' and by 'rulings' all rulings cannot be meant because they are infinite and impossible for a human to encompass. But in the first definition, 'knowledge of the soul's rights and duties' and here 'all rights and duties' can be meant, because by 'rights and duties' not absolute rights and duties are meant, but those rights and duties that pertain to the soul, and the soul, in its religious life, can acquire knowledge of all of them. Therefore the objection to the second definition does not apply to the first definition. Also the application of the word... Second objection: The author said that 'rights and duties' have five probabilities. The objection is that in definitions, using words that admit multiple meanings is ugly and not commendable when there is no indicator specifying a meaning. This is the case here, so this definition is not commendable. Answer (1): According to the early scholars, this is not ugly, and this definition is from the early scholars. Answer (2): The use of a word that admits multiple meanings in definitions is ugly only when only one of those meanings is intended and there is no indicator specifying that meaning. In that case ugliness is necessary because ignorance in the definition would be necessary. If the word admits multiple meanings and all meanings can be intended, then using such a word in definitions is not ugly. Here, leaving aside two of the five probabilities because they do not encompass all divisions, the remaining three can be intended. That is, the addressee has a choice to take whichever meaning, so there is no ugliness in the definition.
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement of the objections made by Sharh al-Taftazani on the definitions of fiqh. The author (Sadr al-Shariah) is defended against two objections. The first objection concerns the term 'ahkam' in the definition 'al-ilm bi al-ahkam'. It is argued that if it means all rulings, it is impossible; if specific rulings, they are indeterminate; if unspecified, it is unknown. The responses clarify that the definition transmitted from Imam Abu Hanifa is not subject to this objection in the same way, as the first definition uses 'maliha wa ma alayha' which relates to the soul's own actions, which are finite and knowable. The second objection relates to the phrase 'maliha wa ma alayha' having five possible meanings, making the definition ambiguous. The response explains that when multiple meanings can all be intended, and the context allows any of them, it is not a defect. The definition is from the early Hanafi scholars (qadama) and is therefore acceptable.

Key Terms
التعریف الثانی (al-ta'rīf al-thānī): The second definition of fiqh, often attributed to the Shafi'is, as 'knowledge of the rulings'.
محتمل للمعانی (muḥtamil li-l-ma‘ānī): A word that can bear multiple meanings, potentially causing ambiguity in a definition.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why did the author of al-Tawdih not mention the first objection on the first definition?
A) Because the first definition is from Imam Abu Hanifa, so he refrained out of respect.
B) Because the objection only applies to the second definition.
C) Because the first definition is not about knowledge of rulings.
D) Because he forgot to mention it.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, a definition is not invalidated by ambiguous wording if all possible meanings are legally valid and the addressee has discretion.

Distinguishing Effective and Declarative Causes to Defend the Eternity of Hukm
Refutation of Mu'tazila on temporality of hukm

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi distinction between illah mu'aththirah and illah mu'arrafah to defend the position that hukm is qadim.

Core Idea
The ruling is eternal; temporal acts are signs, not causes, of divine command.

In this passage, the author refutes the Mu'tazila claim that the Shar'i ruling must be created because it arises from new acts. By distinguishing effective and declarative causes, the Hanafi-Ash'ari school shows that human actions are merely indicators of pre-eternal divine rulings, not causes that produce them. This distinction preserves the qadim (eternal) nature of Allah's speech.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Mu'tazila argument
Recognize that the Mu'tazila claim: (1) the ruling is an effect of a temporal cause, (2) if the cause is temporal, the effect must be temporal, therefore (3) the ruling is temporal (hadith).
This is a syllogism: minor premise (ruling is caused by temporal acts) + major premise (temporal cause → temporal effect) → conclusion (ruling is temporal).
2
Accept the minor premise, reject the major
The Hanafi response accepts that rulings are related to temporal acts (الصغریٰ مسلم), but rejects that any temporal cause necessarily yields a temporal effect.
The key is the type of cause: not all temporal causes produce temporal effects.
3
Distinguish the two types of cause
Effective cause (علة مؤثرة): produces the existence of its effect; if it is temporal, the effect is temporal. Declarative cause (علة معرفة): merely indicates the pre-existing effect; its temporality does not affect the effect's eternity.
Example: the world's temporality indicates the eternal Creator; similarly, acts indicate eternal rulings.
4
Apply the distinction to the Mu'tazila argument
Shar'i actions are declarative causes, not effective causes. Therefore, the major premise (temporal cause → temporal effect) does not apply. The ruling remains qadim.
The real effective cause of the ruling is Allah's eternal attribute, not the human act.
5
Conclude that the ruling is qadim
Since the only plausible objection (the Mu'tazila syllogism) fails, the eternality of the ruling stands confirmed.
This aligns with the Hanafi-Ash'ari belief that Allah's speech is uncreated.

Source Text

تعلق اب ہوا ہے لہذا جب ثابت ہو گیا کہ نفس حکم قدیم ہے تو آپ کی دلیل باطل ہو گئی (۲) دوسری دلیل کا جواب یہ ہے آپ نے یہ فرمایا کہ حکم معلول ہوتا ہے علت حادثہ کے ساتھ اور جب علت حادث ہو تو معلول بھی حادث ہوتا ہے اس کا جواب یہ ہے کہ ہم آپ کا صغری تو تسلیم کرتے ہیں کہ حکم معلل بالعلت الحادثہ ہوتا ہے لیکن کبری تسلیم نہیں کرتے کہ جب علت حادث ہو تو معلول (حکم) بھی حادث ہوتا ہے بلکہ ہم عرض کرتے ہیں کہ علت کی دو قسمیں ہیں (۱) علت مؤثرہ و موجہ، یہ وہ علت ہے کہ جو وجود اور ثبوت حکم ( معلول ) میں اثر کرتی ہے یعنی علت کی وجہ سے ہی حکم اور معلول کو وجود حاصل ہوتا ہے اور معلول کا وجود اسی علت پر موقوف ہوتا ہے یہ جو ضابطہ ہے کہ اگر علت حادث ہو تو معلول بھی حادث ہوتا ہے اسی علت مؤثرہ کے بارے میں ہے اور آئمیں کوئی شک نہیں ہے کہ علت مؤثر ہ اگر حادث ہو تو حکم بھی حادث ہوتا ہے (۲) دوسری قسم علت معرفہ علت معرفہ وہ ہے جو معلول کے وجود اور ثبوت پر صرف نشانی و علامت و آمارۃ ہوتی ہے معلول کا وجود اس پر موقوف نہیں ہوتا وہ معلول کے وجود اور ثبوت میں مؤثر نہیں ہوتی اس کے ساتھ صرف معلول کا وجود معلوم ہو جاتا ہے اور ضابطہ مذکور و ( اگر علت حادثہ ہوتو حکم اور معلول بھی حادث ہوتا ہے ) علت معرفہ کے بارے میں نہیں ہے بلکہ علت معرفہ حادث ہوتو یہ حکم قدیم کیلئے نشانی اور علامت بن سکتی ہے جیسے عالم حادث ہے اور یہ علت اور امارت ہے وجود صانع پر اور صانع ( معلول ) قدیم ہے اور حکم شرعی کی جتنی بھی علل ہیں وہ سب علت کی دوسری قسم میں سے ہیں کیونکہ عمل شرعیہ اپنے حکم کیلئے صرف نشانی اور علامت بنتی ہیں یہ حکم شرعی میں مؤثر نہیں ہوتیں کیونکہ مؤثر حقیقی اللہ تعالی کی ذات ہے جب ثابت ہوا کہ عمل شرعیہ معرفات و امارات و علامات ہیں موجبات اور مؤثرات نہیں ہیں تو ان علل شرعیہ کے حادث ہونے سے حکم کا حادث ہونا ثابت نہیں ہوگا کیونکہ علت معرفہ کا حدوث حدوث معلول کو یستلزم نہیں ہے لہذا آپ کی یہ دلیل کہ حکم چونکہ معلل بالحادث ہوتا ہے لہذا یہ حکم خود بھی حادث ہوگا ناقص ہے اور اس سے دعوی ثابت نہیں ہوتا جب حدوث حکم کی دونوں دلیلین باطل ہو گئیں تو حکم کا قدیم ہونا ثابت ہو گیا اور یہی ہمارا مدعی تھا فاندفع اعتراض المعتزلة
The discussion is now connected. So when it has been proven that the ruling itself is eternal, your proof becomes invalid. (2) The answer to the second proof: you said that the ruling is an effect caused by a temporal cause, and when the cause is temporal, the effect is also temporal. The answer is that we accept your minor premise that the ruling is caused by a temporal cause, but we do not accept the major premise that when the cause is temporal, the effect (ruling) is also temporal. Rather, we say that there are two types of cause: (1) effective cause (علة مؤثرة) and (2) declarative cause (علة معرفة). The effective cause is that which produces existence and confirmation of the ruling (effect); that is, the ruling and effect exist only because of the cause, and existence of the effect depends on it. The principle that if the cause is temporal, the effect is also temporal applies only to the effective cause, and there is no doubt that if the effective cause is temporal, the ruling is also temporal. (2) The second type is the declarative cause, which is merely a sign and indicator of the existence and confirmation of the effect. The existence of the effect does not depend on it; it does not affect the existence and confirmation of the effect. It only makes the existence of the effect known. The aforementioned principle (if the cause is temporal, the ruling and effect are also temporal) does not apply to the declarative cause. Rather, if the declarative cause is temporal, it can be a sign for the eternal ruling, like the world is temporal and it is a cause and sign for the existence of the Creator, while the Creator (effect) is eternal. All the causes of Shar'i rulings are of this second type, because Shar'i actions are only signs for their rulings; they are not effective in the Shar'i ruling, because the real effective cause is the Essence of Allah Almighty. When it is proven that Shar'i actions are declarative signs and indicators, not effective causes, then the temporality of these Shar'i causes does not prove the temporality of the ruling, because the occurrence of a declarative cause does not necessitate the occurrence of its effect. Therefore, your proof that the ruling is caused by a temporal cause and therefore it is also temporal is incomplete and does not prove the claim. When both proofs of the temporality of the ruling are invalid, the eternity of the ruling is proven, and this was our claim. Thus, the objection of the Mu'tazila is repelled.
Source pages p. 172

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazila argument that the Shar'i ruling (hukm) must be created (hadith) because it arises from temporal causes (such as human actions). The Hanafi-Ash'ari position is that the ruling itself is eternal (qadim) as an attribute of Allah, while the actions of the servant are merely signs (علة معرفة) that indicate the pre-existing ruling, not effective causes (علة مؤثرة) that produce it. The author distinguishes two types of cause: effective cause (which necessitates its effect in time) and declarative cause (which only indicates the effect without causing it). For example, the temporality of the world indicates the existence of the eternal Creator; similarly, temporal acts like prayer at the time of zawaal indicate the pre-existing obligation, but do not create the obligation. Therefore, the ruling remains eternal, and the Mu'tazila objection is invalid.

Key Terms
علة مؤثرة (illah mu'aththirah): A cause that effectively produces the existence of its effect; if it is temporal, the effect must be temporal.
علة معرفة (illah mu'arrafah): A cause that merely indicates the existence of its effect without being effective; its temporality does not imply the temporality of the effect.
حادث (hadith): Something that comes into existence after not being; temporally created.
قدیم (qadim): Eternal, without beginning; an attribute of Allah and His speech.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Effective cause (علة مؤثرة)
B) Declarative cause (علة معرفة)
C) Both equally
D) Neither

Key Takeaway
The temporality of the cause does not imply the temporality of the effect when the cause is merely declarative, not effective.

Distinguishing the Three Ash'ari Positions on Wad'i Rulings: Taftazani's Error
Clarifying the three Ash'ari schools regarding wad'i rulings

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three Ash'ari groups regarding hukm wad'i and explain why Taftazani's conflation leads to invalid objections.

Core Idea
Taftazani's critique collapses when we realise the Ash'aris hold three distinct positions on wad'i rulings, not two.

In Hanafi usul al-fiqh, understanding the precise positions of other schools is crucial for evaluating critiques. This lesson examines an error made by the commentator Taftazani when he conflated two distinct Ash'ari views on declaratory rulings (hukm wad'i). By correctly identifying the three Ash'ari groups, we see that Taftazani's objections to Sadr al-Shariah's Al-Tawdih are based on a misunderstanding.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three Ash'ari groups on hukm wad'i
From the passage, list the three positions: (1) those who do not call wad'i a hukm, (2) those who call it hukm and include it under iqtidha, and (3) those who call it hukm and treat it as a separate category.
The passage mentions groups 1 and 3; group 2 is implied by the existence of three groups.
2
Recognize Taftazani's error
Taftazani erroneously merged groups 1 and 3 into one, thinking they were the same. This led him to misread the author's argument.
This error is the root of his subsequent objections.
3
Understand how the error generates false objections
Because Taftazani conflated two positions, his objections target a straw man. Once the correct taxonomy is applied, the objections lose their force.
For example, an argument valid against group 1 may not apply to group 3.
4
Appreciate the correct classification as a key to dispute resolution
Realizing there are three groups dissolves Taftazani's critique automatically. This shows the importance of accurate doxography in usul debates.
The passage states that after this disclosure, the objections 'disappear by themselves.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The commentator (of Misk al-Mulayh) says: The commentator (Taftazani) considered the first group (those who do not name wad'i as hukm) and the third group (those who name it hukm and include it in iqtidha) as one group. This is a clear mistake. And from the beginning, the objections that the commentator Taftazani raises against the author, all are based on his own misunderstanding. After revealing this fact (that there are three Ash'ari groups), these objections automatically disappear.
Source pages p. 175

Commentary
This passage identifies a key error in Taftazani's critique of Sadr al-Shariah's Al-Tawdih. The author of Misk al-Mulayh clarifies that the Ash'aris are not two but three groups regarding wad'i (declaratory) rulings: (1) those who do not recognize wad'i as a ruling at all, (2) those who recognize it but include it implicitly under 'iqtidha' (requirement), and (3) those who recognize it explicitly as a distinct category. Taftazani mistakenly merged groups 1 and 3, leading him to misunderstand the author's entire argument. This clarification shows that once we realize there are three groups, the objections Taftazani raises later become irrelevant because they are premised on a false conflation.

Key Terms
حكم وضعي (hukm wad'i): A declaratory ruling that indicates something as a cause, condition, impediment, or correct/incorrect, according to some Ash'ari groups. It is a category of legal ruling in usul al-fiqh.
اقتضاء (iqtidha): Requirement; a category of hukm wad'i according to some Ash'aris, under which conditions and impediments are subsumed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Correctly classifying the three Ash'ari positions on hukm wad'i dissolves Taftazani's objections and underscores the importance of precise doxography in usul debates.

Rational Ethics and the Scope of Fiqh: Hanafi vs. Ash'ari
Explaining the two positions on husn and qubh and their inclusion in fiqh

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the Hanafi and Ash'ari positions on the source of moral value (husn and qubh) and evaluate how each affects the definition of fiqh.

Core Idea
For Hanafis, rationally known ethics fall outside fiqh; for Ash'aris, all ethics is legal.

This passage addresses a foundational dispute in usul al-fiqh: where does moral value come from? The Hanafi and Ash'ari schools hold divergent views, and this difference has direct implications for how we define the scope of fiqh itself. Understanding this dispute clarifies why fiqh is limited to revealed rulings in the Hanafi tradition.

Guided Path
1
Separate rational vs. revealed ethics
Identify that the dispute hinges on whether acts like generosity are good by nature (rational) or only because revelation declares them good.
Examples: generosity (joud), humility (tawadu'), arrogance (takabbur).
2
Apply the Hanafi frame to the definition of fiqh
Posit that fiqh deals only with acts whose rulings depend on revelation; thus rational ethics are excluded from fiqh and belong to ethics (ilm al-akhlaq).
The definition of fiqh as 'knowledge of practical shar'i rulings' is thus 'mani'' (comprehensive yet exclusive).
3
Apply the Ash'ari frame and note the objection
If all ethics is revelational, then rationally known ethics would be forced into fiqh, making the definition too broad and including subjects like ethics and psychology.
Ash'aris hold that intellect is incapable of independent moral judgment.
4
Evaluate which view preserves the integrity of fiqh
The text indicates the Hanafi view is preferred because it keeps fiqh focused on revealed rulings, avoiding category confusion.
The tarjih is implicit: the Hanafi view makes the definition 'mani' (sound).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله فيدخل الخ مصنف نے متن میں تعریف فقہ پر اعتراض کیا تھا شارح اس کی تشریح کر رہے ہیں حاصل تشریح یہ ہے کہ ہر چیز کے حسن اور فتح کے شرعی اور عقلی ہونے کے بارے میں دو گروہ بہین (۱) گروہ احناف و جمہور معتز به (۲) گروہ اشاعرہ ،گروہ احناف و جمہور معتزلہ کامذہب یہ ہے کہ بعض افعال کا حسن اور قبح عقلی ہوتا ہے یعنی عقل کے ذریعہ پہچانا جاتا ہے اور بعض افعال کا حسن اور فتح مدرک بالعقل نہیں ہوتا بلکہ موقوف على الشرع ہوتا ہے جن افعال کا حسن اور فتح عقلی ہوتا ہے وہ فقہ میں داخل نہیں بلکہ علم الاخلاق میں سے ہے لہذا یہ افعال الشرعیہ کی قید سے نکل جائیں گے مثلا حسن جود ، حسن تواضع ، فتح تکبر و غیره، کیونکہ یہ افعال موقوف على الشرع نہیں اور الشرعیہ سے مراد ما توقف علی الشرع ہے اور جن افعال کا حسن اور فتح شرعی ہے وہ فقہ میں داخل رہیں گے اور الشرعیہ کی قید سے خارج نہ ہونگے لہذا ہمارے نزدیک تعریف فقہ صحیح اور مانع ہوگی (۲) گروہ اشاعرہ کا مذہب یہ ہے کہ ہر چیز کا حسن اور فتح شرعی یعنی موقوف علی الشرع ہوتا ہے کیونکہ منقل احکام کے ادراک اور علم حاصل کرنے میں بے بس ہے جب ان کے نزدیک ہر چیز کا حسن اور فتح شرعی ہے تو ان پر اشکال ہوگا کہ آپ کے مذہب کے مطابق فقہ کی تعریف مانع نہ ہوگی علم الاخلاق اور علم الوجدانیات اس میں داخل ہو جائیں گے کیونکہ وہ الشرعیہ بمعنی ما يتوقف على الشرع میں داخل
His statement: 'Thus enters...' The author had objected to the definition of fiqh in the text; the commentator is explaining it. The summary of the explanation is that regarding the shari and rational nature of the goodness (husn) and badness (qubh) of everything, there are two groups: (1) The group of the Hanafis and the majority of the Mu'tazila, (2) The group of the Ash'aris. The school of the Hanafis and the majority of the Mu'tazila is that the goodness and badness of some acts are rational, i.e., recognized by reason, and the goodness and badness of some acts are not perceived by reason but rather depend on the Sharia. Acts whose goodness and badness are rational are not included in fiqh but are part of ethics (ilm al-akhlaq). Therefore, these acts will be excluded by the restriction of al-shar'iyyah. For example, the goodness of generosity, the goodness of humility, the badness of arrogance, etc., because these acts do not depend on the Sharia, and al-shar'iyyah means that which depends on the Sharia. And acts whose goodness and badness are shari will remain in fiqh and will not be excluded by the restriction of al-shar'iyyah. So according to us, the definition of fiqh is correct and comprehensive (mani'). (2) The school of the Ash'aris is that the goodness and badness of everything is shari, i.e., dependent on the Sharia, because the intellect is incapable of perceiving and knowing the rulings. When according to them the goodness and badness of everything is shari, then they face an objection: according to your school, the definition of fiqh will not be comprehensive (mani'); ethics (ilm al-akhlaq) and introspective sciences (ilm al-wijdaniyyat) will enter into it, because they are included in al-shar'iyyah in the meaning of that which depends on the Sharia.
Source pages p. 190

Commentary
This passage addresses the definition of fiqh and the objection that it should exclude rationally known ethical values. The commentator explains that there are two views: the Hanafi/Mu'tazili view distinguishes between rational ethics (known by reason, e.g., goodness of generosity) and legal ethics (dependent on revelation, e.g., obligation of prayer). Only the latter falls within the definition of fiqh because fiqh deals with 'al-shar'iyyah' (that which is based on revelation). The Ash'ari view holds that all ethics are dependent on revelation, so rationally known ethics would also be considered shar'iyyah, causing the definition to include subjects like ethics and psychology, which are not traditionally part of fiqh. The text thus defends the Hanafi definition by showing that it correctly excludes non-legal ethical knowledge.

Key Terms
حسن (husn): Goodness or moral value of an act
قبح (qubh): Badness or moral disvalue of an act
عقلي ('aqli): Rational, knowable by intellect independently of revelation
شرعي (shar'i): Legal, based on revelation

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, why is the act of generosity (joud) not part of fiqh?
A) Because it is a recommended act.
B) Because its goodness is known by reason, not revelation.
C) Because it is not mentioned in the Quran.
D) Because it is an ethical duty.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi definition of fiqh is 'mani'' because it excludes rationally known ethics, preserving fiqh as a discipline of revealed rulings.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement

8 min
Imam Razi's Qualifier in the Definition of Fiqh
Defending Imam Razi's qualifier against criticism from al-Tawdih

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why Imam Razi added a qualifier to the definition of fiqh and evaluate the Hanafi argument that such knowledge is included.

Core Idea
Fiqh definitions differ: Shafi'is exclude necessary knowledge; Hanafis include all evidence-based rulings.

This passage delves into a methodological dispute between the Shafi'i and Hanafi schools regarding the definition of fiqh. Imam Razi added a qualifier to exclude knowledge that is necessarily known from religion, but the author of al-Tawdih and the Hanafi position argue that such knowledge is an integral part of fiqh. Understanding this dispute clarifies the scope of jurisprudence and the role of evidence in defining the field.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main positions
State the Shafi'i (Imam Razi) position: fiqh is knowledge of non-evident rulings, excluding necessities. State the Hanafi (author of al-Tawdih) position: such knowledge is part of fiqh because it is derived from evidences.
Imam Razi's definition: 'al-ilm bi al-ahkam al-shar'iyyah al-ghayr al-ma'luma min al-din bi al-darurah'.
2
Understand the qualifier
What does the qualifier 'al-ghayr al-ma'luma min al-din bi al-darurah' exclude? It excludes matters known necessarily from religion, like the essence of prayer. Why? Because Shafi'is argue that such knowledge is not considered fiqh.
The qualifier is meant to narrow the definition to avoid calling a layperson a faqih.
3
Analyze the Hanafi objection
The author of al-Tawdih objects that the qualifier is useless and that the obligation of prayer is fiqh. The commentator provides six arguments to refute this, including that jurists discuss obligation using evidence, and that self-evidence varies by person.
Key arguments: (1) Razi's own phrase shows exclusion leads to calling one a faqih; (2) obligation of prayer is discussed in fiqh; (3) excluding self-evident matters wastes knowledge; (4) self-evidence is subjective; (5) jurists use proofs, so it's theoretical; (6) distinguish essence (self-evident) from obligation (theoretical).
4
Evaluate the defense
Assess the strength of the commentator's defense. The qualifier is necessary within Shafi'i terminology, and the principle 'la mushahata fi al-istilah' allows each school to define terms. However, Hanafis disagree on substance.
The commentator does not reject the Shafi'i position but defends it as consistent.
5
Apply the principle of la mushahata
Understand that disputes over definitions are not substantive but terminological. The Hanafi position is that fiqh includes all rulings derived from evidences, even those that become self-evident to some. The Thamarah al-khilaf is that a jurist knowing only obligation of prayer is a faqih in Hanafi but not Shafi'i view.
This principle prevents endless arguments and respects school-specific conventions.

(۱) مصنف کا رد صیح ہے کیونکہ امام رازی نے جو عبارت الحصول میں ذکر کی ہے وہ یہ ہے انما قولنا لا يعلم كونها من الدين ضرورة احتراز عن العلم بوجوب الصلوه والصوم مثلا فان ذالك لا يسمى فقها ابرازی کی یہ عبارت دلالت کر رہی ہے کہ اگر یہ قید ذکر نہ کی جائے تو علم وجوب صلوۃ و الصوم کو فقہ کے ساتھ موسوم کرنا پڑیگا اور انہیں فقہ کہا جائے گا تو ظاہر بات ہے جب ان کو فقہ کہا جائے گا تو ان کے عالم کو بھی فقیہ کہنا پڑے گا تو اعتراض مصنف صحیح ہے (۲) مصنف کا اعتراض اسی وجہ سے ہے کہ وجوب صلوۃ وصوم و غیرہ کو فقہ سے خارج کرنا درست نہیں ہے کیونکہ یہ فقہ میں سے ہیں کیونکہ فقہاء وجوب سلوۃ وغیرہ سے فقہ میں بحث کرتے ہیں (۳) جمیع فنون میں بعض مسائل بدیہیہ ہوتے ہیں اور بعض استدلالی و نظری اگر قید لگا کر مسائل بدیہیہ کو خارج کر دیا جائے تو علم کا ایک حصہ وافر ضائع ہو جائے گا اس لیے امام رازی کی قید درست نہیں ہے (۴) بد بھیات کا اخراج اس وجہ بھی صحیح نہیں ہے کیونکہ بداہت و نظر باعتبار الاشخاص مختلف ہوتے ہیں ایک شخص کے ہاں ایک چیز بدیہی ہے لیکن بالنظر الی شخص اخر وہی چیز نظری ہوتی ہے لہذا وجوب صلوۃ وغیرہ عند البعض بدیہی ہیں لیکن ممکن ہے عند البعض نظری ہوں (۵) فقہاء وجوب صلوۃ وجوب صوم کو دلائل سے ثابت کرتے ہیں اس سے ثابت ہوا کہ ان کا علم نظری ہے بدیہی نہیں ورنہ دلائل فقہاء عبث ہو جائیں گے (۶) یہاں دو چیز میں ہیں ایک ہے نفس صلوۃ و نفس صوم. دوسرا ہے وجوب صلوۃ وجوب صوم وغیرہ اول کا علم بدیہی ہے لیکن ثانی کا علم نظری و استدلالی ہے اول کا اخراج من الفقہ تو درست ہے لیکن ثانی کا اخراج صحیح نہیں ہے کیونکہ وجوب صلوة وجوب صوم کے مباحث فقہ میں مذکور ہیں بلکہ امھات مسائل فقہ میں سے ہیں متفکر ۔
Misk al-Malih: They did not understand the purpose of this qualifier, rather they considered the unintended as Imam Razi's intention and objected to it. It is not said that this is not at all the purpose—that if they are not excluded, then the one who knows them would have to be called a faqih. So the author (of al-Tawdih) criticizing Imam Razi's terminology? The commentator (Sharh) further explains that when according to the Shafi'is the knowledge of the necessities of religion is not fiqh, therefore to exclude them a qualifier must be in the definition of fiqh. Hence Imam Razi placed this qualifier. Therefore the statement 'fa-aqulu hadha al-qayd da'i' (I say this qualifier is useless) is wrong; rather this qualifier is beneficial and exclusive. And the author's statement 'fa al-'ilmu bi wujubi al-salat wa al-sawm min al-fiqh' (the knowledge of the obligation of prayer and fasting is from fiqh) is also wrong, because the Shafi'is according to their terminology do not call it fiqh. So according to their terminology, saying that these are from fiqh would not be correct. And they cannot be objected to by saying 'why did you make this terminology? We do not accept your terminology' because the rule is 'la mushahata fi al-istilah' (no dispute in terminology). Hence the author's refutation of Imam Razi is not correct. Answer: (1) The author's refutation is correct because the phrase that Imam Razi mentioned in al-Husul is: 'Innama qawluna la yu'lamu kawnuha min al-din daruratan ihtiradan 'an al-'ilmi bi wujubi al-salat wa al-sawm mathalan fa inna dhalika la yusamma fiqhan.' Imam Razi's phrase indicates that if this qualifier is not mentioned, then the knowledge of the obligation of prayer and fasting would have to be designated as fiqh and they would be called fiqh. So it is obvious that when they are called fiqh, then the one who knows them would also have to be called a faqih. So the author's objection is correct. (2) The author's objection is precisely because excluding the obligation of prayer, fasting, etc. from fiqh is not correct, as they are from fiqh, because jurists discuss the obligation of prayer etc. in fiqh. (3) In all disciplines, some issues are self-evident and some are inferential and theoretical. If by placing a qualifier self-evident issues are excluded, a large portion of knowledge would be wasted. Therefore Imam Razi's qualifier is not correct. (4) The exclusion of self-evident matters is also not correct because self-evidence and theory vary from person to person. For one person a thing is self-evident, but with regard to another person the same thing is theoretical. Hence the obligation of prayer etc. are self-evident for some, but it is possible that for others they are theoretical. (5) Jurists prove the obligation of prayer and fasting with evidences. This proves that their knowledge is theoretical, not self-evident, otherwise the evidences of the jurists would be futile. (6) Here there are two things: one is the essence of prayer and the essence of fasting; the second is the obligation of prayer and obligation of fasting, etc. The knowledge of the first is self-evident, but the knowledge of the second is theoretical and inferential. Excluding the first from fiqh is correct, but excluding the second is not correct because the discussions of the obligation of prayer and obligation of fasting are mentioned in fiqh; rather they are among the foundational issues of fiqh. And it is considered.
Source pages p. 193

Commentary
This passage is a dispute regarding the definition of fiqh. The author of al-Tawdih criticizes Imam Razi's addition of a qualifier in the definition of fiqh to exclude knowledge that is necessarily known from religion, such as the obligation of prayer and fasting, because according to the Shafi'is such knowledge is not considered fiqh. The commentator of Misk al-Malih defends Imam Razi by arguing that the qualifier is necessary according to Shafi'i terminology, and the objection from al-Tawdih misunderstands the purpose. The commentator then provides six arguments to show that the author's objection is not valid, including that the knowledge of obligation is not self-evident but theoretical, and that excluding it would be incorrect. This illustrates the Hanafi concern for preserving the scope of fiqh to include all legal rulings derived from evidences, even those that may seem obvious to some.

Key Terms
قید (qayd): A restrictive qualifier added to a definition to exclude certain items.
بدیہی (badihi): Self-evident knowledge that requires no proof.
نظری (nazari): Theoretical knowledge that requires inference and proof.
لَا مُشَاحَةَ فِي الِاصْطِلَاحِ (la mushahata fi al-istilah): There is no dispute over terminology; each school may define terms as they see fit.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) It excludes all self-evident knowledge, making fiqh purely theoretical.
B) It is necessary according to Shafi'i terminology to exclude knowledge that is necessarily known from religion.
C) It prevents non-fuqaha from being called faqih.
D) It was required by the author of al-Tawdih.

Key Takeaway
The definition of fiqh varies between schools; the Hanafi view includes all evidence-based rulings, while the Shafi'i excludes necessarily known matters, and the dispute is resolved by recognizing terminological differences.

The Meaning of 'Ilm in the Definition of Fiqh: Faculty or Perception?
Dispute on whether 'ilm in fiqh definition means faculty

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on the meaning of 'ilm in the definition of fiqh and identify the condition under which 'ilm can mean faculty (malaka).

Core Idea
'Ilm means actual perception when its object is mentioned; faculty only when absolute.

This lesson unpacks a key dispute in usul al-fiqh: does the word 'ilm (knowledge) in the definition of fiqh refer to the faculty of deduction (malaka) or to actual perception? The author argues it means actual perception, while the commentator Taftazani objects. Understanding this nuance is crucial for a precise definition of fiqh as a discipline.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contested term
Is 'ilm in the definition of fiqh interpreted as malaka (faculty) or as actual perception? The definition is 'knowledge of the practical shar'i rulings from detailed evidences'.
The object 'al-ahkam' is present.
2
Analyze the linguistic principle
When the object of 'ilm is mentioned, it signifies actual perception. Here 'ilm is followed by 'al-ahkam', so it should mean actual knowledge.
This is a Hanafi usul principle.
3
Examine Taftazani's objection
Taftazani gives two proofs: (1) custom in defining sciences uses 'ilm for faculty, (2) analogy with 'ilm and hayat (life) shows 'ilm can mean cause.
Understand each proof.
4
Evaluate the Hanafi response
The response counters that the object's presence forces the meaning to actual perception, and the analogy fails because the object is mentioned in the definition.
Also, knowledge from detailed evidences is actual knowledge, not faculty.
5
Recognize the thamarah al-khilaf
The correct interpretation excludes malaka from the definition of fiqh; the definition is clarified as actual knowledge plus a separate faculty.
This distinguishes the fiqh definition from definitions of other sciences.

And we do not accept that the word 'ilm has no indication for the [؟]. The author stated the fourth reason for not intending [؟] that it is not correct to use 'ilm and intend from it [؟]. The commentator Taftazani refutes this, saying that 'ilm can be used to intend [؟]. He gave two proofs: (1) [؟] means that faculty by which a person is able to deduce particulars, and using the word 'ilm to mean that faculty is widespread in custom. For example, when sciences are defined and it is said 'ilm such and such, for the researchers 'ilm here means that faculty, not the act of perception, and it is also called a craft. (2) The second proof: the investigators say that 'ilm and life are similar and the reason for similarity is that both are a means and cause of [؟]. Now see, here 'ilm in the sense of faculty and [؟] means that faculty and disposition is the cause of [؟] and [؟] means actual knowledge. If 'ilm also meant actual knowledge, then the meaning would be knowledge is the cause of knowledge, which is absurd. Response: The response to Taftazani's refutation is that 'ilm can intend [؟] and faculty only when the word 'ilm is used absolutely and its object (the known) is not mentioned. If the object is mentioned, then 'ilm does not mean faculty but actual perception. Here in the definition of fiqh, the object 'al-ahkam' is mentioned, so faculty cannot be intended; rather it is actual perception. And in the case of 'al-ilm al-hayah', since the word 'ilm is absolute, it can mean faculty. Also, here (in the definition of fiqh) 'ilm cannot intend faculty because the knowledge from detailed evidences is itself actual knowledge, not faculty. Therefore the author's statement that here 'ilm cannot mean faculty is absolutely correct. Benefit: The word 'ilm is used in books with three meanings: (1) tasdiq and perception, (2) issues (masa'il), as in 'the science of [؟] has been compiled', (3) faculty and disposition, which is intended in definitions of sciences.
Source pages p. 201

Commentary
This passage presents a dispute about the meaning of the word 'ilm in the definition of fiqh. The original author (likely Sadr al-Shariah) argued that 'ilm cannot mean malaka (faculty) in the definition because the object (al-ahkam) is mentioned. Taftazani objects, giving two proofs: first, common usage in defining sciences uses 'ilm to mean the faculty of deduction; second, an analogy with 'ilm and hayat (life) suggests both are causes. The response is that when the object of 'ilm is mentioned, it necessarily means actual perception. In the definition of fiqh, 'ilm is followed by 'al-ahkam', so it means actual knowledge, not the faculty. Additionally, the knowledge derived from detailed evidences is actual knowledge. Therefore Taftazani's objection is rejected. A benefit then lists the three meanings of 'ilm: (1) perception, (2) particular issues, (3) faculty. This clarifies the correct interpretation.

Key Terms
علم ('ilm): In the definition of fiqh, 'ilm means actual perception/knowledge (tasdiq/idrak), not the faculty, because the object is mentioned.
ملکہ (malaka): The faculty of deduction that enables a jurist to derive rulings from evidence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) When it is used absolutely without mentioning the object.
C) Always.
D) Only in the definition of fiqh.

Key Takeaway
In the definition of fiqh, 'ilm means actual perception of rulings, not the faculty of deduction, because the object 'al-ahkam' is mentioned.

Certainty of Conjectural Rulings: Musawwiba vs Ghayr Musawwiba
Analyzing two interpretations of certainty in qiyasi rulings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the difference between the Musawwiba and Ghayr Musawwiba views on the certainty of qiyasi rulings and apply the Hanafi tarjih based on ijma' and khabar.

Core Idea
A conjectural ruling becomes certain in obligation, not in external reality.

In usul al-fiqh, a ruling derived through qiyas is conjectural (maznun) by nature. However, can it be considered certain (maqtu')? This passage from a commentary on al-Tawdih wa al-Talwih presents two views: the Musawwiba hold that qiyas itself makes the ruling certain in the essence of the matter; the Ghayr Musawwiba argue that ijma' and khabar ahad make acting upon the conjecture obligatory, thus certain in obligation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions
Clearly state the two schools: Musawwiba (every mujtahid is correct) and Ghayr Musawwiba (only one is correct, but acting on dominant conjecture is obligatory).
Musawwiba base certainty on qiyas; Ghayr Musawwiba base it on external evidence.
2
Analyze the Musawwiba proof
Examine their qiyas: if a ruling is known to be conjectural for the mujtahid, it is known to be certain in nafs al-amr. Identify the logical steps.
This view makes the ruling itself certain in external reality, not just in obligation.
3
Analyze the Ghayr Musawwiba proof
Understand their reliance on ijma' and khabar ahad to establish wujub al-'amal as a decisive divine command, making the ruling certain in obligation.
The certainty here is in the obligation to act, not in the ruling's correspondence to reality.
4
Evaluate objections and Hanafi tarjih
Consider the objections (fiqh as knowledge of rulings vs obligation, certainty vs evidence) and see how the Hanafi school answers them, leading to the mufta bihi position.
The text defends the Ghayr Musawwiba view more extensively, likely the relied-upon position.

یعنی جس حکم پر غلبہ من مجتہد ہو جائے تو وہ با النظر الی حکم طن الدلیل قطعا ثابت ہوتا ہے اگر چہ نفس الامر میں فی علم اللہ ثابت نہ ہوتو پھر بھی وہ حکم مظنون با انظر الی الدلیل قطعی ہو جائے گا لكن يلزم على الاول یہاں سے تقریر ثانی پر اشکال کر رہے ہیں کہ تقریر ثانی جو کہ فرقہ غیر مصوبہ کی طرف سے تھی کی دو شقیں تھیں دونوں پر اشکال ہے اول شق پر یہ اشکال لازم آئے گا کہ جب یہ بات معلوم ہوگئی کہ مظنون مجتہد واجب العمل ہے تو فقہ وجوب عمل بالا حکام کے علم کا نام ہوگی کیونکہ اس دلیل سے وجوب عمل ثابت ہوتا ہے حالانکہ فقہ تو لمبا الا حکام کو کہتے ہیں نہ کہ علم بوجوب العمل کو دوسری شق پر یہ اشکال لازم آئے گا کہ جو حکم مظنون دلیل سے ثابت ہو وہ قطعی ہوگا اگر چہ وہ دلیل ظنی ہوا اور نفس الامر اور واقع میں وہ حکم قطعی نہ بھی ہو لیکن بالنظر الی الدلیل وہ قطعی ہوگا حالانکہ یہ غلط ہے کیونکہ حکم قطعی تو دہ ہوتا ہے جو نفس الامر اور واقع میں ثابت ہو اور نقیض کا احتمال نہ رکھتا ہو۔ جواب ! پہلی شق پر جو اشکال کیا گیا ہے اس کا جواب یہ دیا گیا ہے کہ وجوب عمل بغلن الجہد کا مطلب یہ ہے کہ مجتہد یقین اور جزم کرے کہ ظن جس بھر کے وجوب پر دال ہے وہ واجب ہے اور جس چیز کی حرمت پر دال ہے وہ حرام ہے تو اسی کو علم با الاحکام کہا جاتا ہے کیونکہ وجوب اور حرمت احکام میں اور انہی کے علم کو فقہ کہا جاتا ہے شق ثانی کا جواب یہ ہے کہ یہاں علم بالا حکام سے مراد وہ علم ہے جو مقابل ظن ہے تو حکم قطعی اور غیر قطعی سب کو شامل ہے۔
Because we have already said that error occurs in the path and the method, and certainty is in the essence of the ruling. (2) The gist of Sharh Taftazani's answer is that there is no combination of opposites here, but rather the time of the two rulings is different. Because the ruling was conjectural (maznun) before qiyas and will become decisive (maqtu') after qiyas. When we consider qiyas and demonstration (burhan), then the conjectural ruling becomes known and decisive. That qiyas is: 'It is known that this ruling is conjectural for the mujtahid, and everything that is known to be conjectural for the mujtahid is known to be established in the essence of the matter (nafs al-amr) with certainty. Conclusion: This ruling is known to be established in the essence of the matter with certainty.' So the ruling was conjectural before considering this qiyas and became known and decisive after considering the qiyas, hence there is no combination of opposites. This exposition is according to the school of the Musawwiba. As for the assumption that only one is correct (al-musib ahad), that is the exposition of the Ghayr Musawwiba. According to them, the exposition will be: When it is established by ijma' and khabar ahad that acting upon the dominant conjecture of the mujtahid is obligatory, then it is as if the Commentator (peace be upon him) said a decisive text (nass qat'i) that 'Every ruling upon which the conjecture of the mujtahid becomes dominant is necessarily obligatory to act upon.' That is, every ruling upon which the conjecture of the mujtahid becomes dominant, even if it is not certainly established in the essence of the matter, but acting upon it becomes obligatory. So in terms of the obligation to act, it is decisive (because the obligation of the Lawgiver is decisive). Or it can be said: 'Every ruling upon which the conjecture of the mujtahid becomes dominant is established with respect to the evidence (dalil).' That is, the ruling upon which the conjecture of the mujtahid becomes dominant is certainly established with respect to the evidence of conjecture, even if it is not established in the essence of the matter in the knowledge of Allah; still that conjectural ruling with respect to the evidence becomes decisive. However, an objection arises on the first. Here they are objecting to the second exposition. The second exposition, which was from the Ghayr Musawwiba, had two branches; both have objections. On the first branch, the objection is: When it is known that the conjectural (maznun) of the mujtahid is obligatory to act upon, then fiqh would be the knowledge of the obligation to act upon the rulings, because this evidence proves the obligation to act, whereas fiqh is knowledge of the rulings themselves, not knowledge of the obligation to act. On the second branch, the objection is: A ruling that is established by conjectural evidence would be decisive, even if the evidence is conjectural and in the essence of the matter and reality that ruling is not decisive, but with respect to the evidence it would be decisive. But this is wrong, because a decisive ruling is only one that is established in the essence of the matter and reality and does not admit the possibility of its opposite. Answer! The answer to the objection on the first branch is: The meaning of 'obligation to act upon conjecture of the mujtahid' is that the mujtahid is certain and asserts that what his conjecture indicates as obligatory is obligatory and what it indicates as prohibited is prohibited. So this is called knowledge of the rulings, because obligation and prohibition are among the rulings, and knowledge of them is called fiqh. The answer to the second branch is: Here, 'knowledge of the rulings' means the knowledge that is opposite to conjecture, so it includes both decisive and non-decisive rulings.
Source pages p. 212

Commentary
This passage is a discussion from a commentary on Al-Tawdih wa al-Talwih regarding the certainty of rulings derived through qiyas. The author presents two interpretations. The first, from the Musawwiba school, argues that the ruling itself becomes certain after qiyas because the qiyas demonstrates that it is certain in the essence of the matter. The second, from the Ghayr Musawwiba, uses a different approach: through ijma' and khabar ahad (which together form a decisive evidence), it is established that acting upon the mujtahid's dominant conjecture is obligatory. This obligation is certain, so the ruling becomes certain in terms of wujub al-'amal. Objections are raised: fiqh is knowledge of rulings, not of obligation; and certainty with respect to evidence is not real certainty. The answers clarify that the obligation to act implies knowledge of the ruling itself, and that 'knowledge' here is used broadly to include both certain and conjectural. The author thus defends the Ghayr Musawwiba position against these objections.

Key Terms
مظنون (maznun): A ruling based on conjecture (zann), not certain in external reality but accepted as binding in practice.
مقطوع (maqtu'): A decisive, certain ruling that admits no doubt; in this context, it refers to the certainty of the obligation to act.
اجماع (ijma'): Consensus of the mujtahidun of the ummah on a ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Ghayr Musawwiba interpretation, what makes the conjectural ruling certain?
A) The ruling itself becomes certain after qiyas.
B) Ijma' and khabar ahad prove that acting upon it is obligatory.
C) The mujtahid is always correct.
D) The ruling is based on a decisive text from Quran or Sunnah.

Key Takeaway
A conjectural ruling from qiyas is considered certain in the obligation to act upon it, not in its correspondence to external reality, due to the combined evidence of ijma' and khabar ahad.

The Role of Ilm al-Khilaf in Fiqh Derivation
Clarifying why ilm al-khilaf is not a direct means to fiqh

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why 'ilm al-khilaf is excluded from tawassul qarib and apply the distinction between derivation and dialectical defense in analyzing a fiqh dispute.

Core Idea
Ilm al-khilaf protects or attacks rulings; it does not derive them directly from the sources.

In studying usul al-fiqh, one must distinguish between the tools that derive rulings and the tools that defend or challenge them. This passage clarifies that 'ilm al-khilaf (the science of juristic disagreement) belongs to the latter category: it is about protecting one's extracted ruling or demolishing the opponent's, not about deriving the ruling in the first place. This distinction is crucial to understanding the proper place of khilaf in the Hanafi tradition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main claim about 'ilm al-khilaf
The passage begins by stating that through 'ilm al-khilaf one does not reach fiqh directly (tawassul qarib). Instead, it serves dialectical purposes: protecting or attacking a ruling.
Note the phrase 'tawassul qarib' — direct means.
2
Understand the two dialectical roles
The dialectician (munazir) is either a responder (mujib) defending his own wad'i ruling or an objector (mu'tarid) attacking the opposing ruling. This shows that 'ilm al-khilaf is not about derivation.
Wad'i ruling here refers to the extracted ruling.
3
Recognize the apparent objection
An objector might say that since 'ilm al-khilaf discusses many fiqh issues and applies its rules to them, it seems to lead to fiqh. This is the prima facie reason to include it.
4
Follow the author's resolution
The author counters by adding the phrase 'ala wajh al-tahqiq (on a factual basis) to 'close reaching' (tawassul qarib), thereby excluding 'ilm al-khilaf. In reality, it is not a direct derivation method even if it deals with fiqh topics.
This distinction is key: apparent inclusion vs. factual exclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخلاف کے ذریعہ سے توصل الی الفقہ بتوصل قریب نہیں ہوتا بلکہ قواعد علم الجدل حکم مستنبط کی محافظت تک یا حکم مستقبط کی مدافعت تک پہنچاتے ہیں خواہ وہ حکم مستنبط من الفقہ ہو یا من غیر الفقہ ہوتو علم الخلاف کی نسبت فقہ اور غیر فقہ کی طرف برابر ہے الہذا افقہ کے ساتھ مختص نہ ہوا فان الجدلی سے یہ بات بیان کی کہ جدلی ( مناظر ) یا مجیب ہوگا تو وہ اپنے حکم وضعی ( وضعا سے حکم مستنبط مراد ہے ) کی محافظت کرے گا یا وہ معترض ہوگا تو وہ حکم وضعی ( حکم مستقبط ) کی مدافعت کرے گا اور اس کو ہدم کرنے کی کوشش کرے گا لہذا علم مناظرہ بتوصل بھا الی الفقہ سے ہی خارج ہے اس کو علی وجہ اتحقیق سے خارج کرنا اخراج المخرج اور تحصیل حاصل بجواب ! الا ان الفقهاء سے جواب دے رہے ہیں کہ علم الخلاف میں اکثر مسائل فقہ کو ذکر کیا گیا ہے اور اس کے قواعد کو مسائل فقہ پر منطبق کیا گیا ہے اور بہت سے نکات خلافیہ کی بنیاد انہی مسائل فقہ پر ہی رکھی گئی ہے تو باعتبار ظاہر تو ہم پیدا ہوتا تھا کہ علم الخلاف موصل الى مسائل الفقہ ہے گویا باعتبار ظاہر علم الخلاف توصل قریب میں داخل تھا۔ اسی تو ہم کے پیش نظر مصنف نے تو صلا قریبا کے ساتھ علی وجہ اتحقیق کی قید کا اضافہ کر کے علم الخلاف کو خارج کیا
Through ilm al-khilaf one does not reach fiqh directly, but rather the rules of the science of dialetics protect the extracted ruling or defend against the ruling being extracted, whether that extracted ruling is from fiqh or from other than fiqh. Thus ilm al-khilaf is equally related to fiqh and non-fiqh, so it is not specific to fiqh. For the dialectician (munazir) will either be a responder (mujib) protecting his own wad'i ruling (meaning the extracted ruling) or an objector (mu'tarid) defending against the wad'i ruling (the ruling to be extracted) and trying to demolish it. Therefore ilm al-munazara is excluded from 'reaching fiqh'. Excluding it on a factual basis is like excluding something already excluded and achieving something already achieved. [Answer:] But the jurists respond that in ilm al-khilaf, most fiqh issues are mentioned and its rules are applied to fiqh issues, and many points of disagreement are based on these fiqh issues. So apparently one would think that ilm al-khilaf leads to fiqh issues; i.e., apparently ilm al-khilaf was included in 'close reaching'. In consideration of this, the author [of the matn] added the phrase 'on a factual basis' to 'close reaching', thereby excluding ilm al-khilaf.
Source pages p. 228

Commentary
This passage is an exposition of the status of 'ilm al-khilaf' (the science of juristic disagreement) in relation to fiqh. The author explains that 'ilm al-khilaf does not directly lead to fiqh (tawassul qarib) because its purpose is to defend or attack a ruling, not to derive it. The dialectician either protects his own ruling (hukm wad'i, i.e., the ruling he has extracted) or attacks the opponent's ruling. Therefore, it is not a direct means to fiqh. However, an objection is raised: since 'ilm al-khilaf frequently discusses fiqh issues and its rules are applied to them, it appears to lead to fiqh. The author responds that the matn's phrase 'ala wajh al-tahqiq (on a factual basis) excludes this apparent inclusion, meaning that in reality, 'ilm al-khilaf is not a direct method of deriving fiqh rulings, even if it deals with fiqh topics. The key distinction is between direct derivation (tawassul qarib) and supporting or refuting rulings.

Key Terms
علم الخلاف (ʿilm al-khilāf): The science of juristic disagreement, focusing on dialectical defense or attack of rulings.
توصل قریب (tawassul qarīb): The direct means by which fiqh rulings are derived from their sources.
حکم وضعی (ḥukm wadʿī): In this context, the extracted ruling (ḥukm mustanbaṭ).
جدل (jadal): Dialectics; the method of argumentation used in ilm al-khilaf.
مناظر (munāẓir): Dialectician; one who engages in munazara (disputation).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is ilm al-khilaf not considered a direct means (tawassul qarib) to fiqh?
A) It only deals with non-fiqh issues.
B) It does not derive rulings but rather defends or attacks them.
C) It is not mentioned by any Hanafi scholar.
D) It is a branch of logic not related to fiqh.

Key Takeaway
Ilm al-khilaf is a science of debate, not derivation; it does not directly produce fiqh rulings but rather defends or critiques them.

Debate on Including Taqlid and Istifta in Usul al-Fiqh
Responding to objection about inclusion of taqlid in usul al-fiqh

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the methodological argument for including taqlid in usul al-fiqh and apply the principle of clarification by opposites (tatabayyan al-ashya' bi-addadiha) to similar usul disputes.

Core Idea
Taqlid is not a core subject of usul al-fiqh; it is mentioned only by way of contrast to ijtihad.

In this passage, the author defends the practice of some scholars who mention taqlid and istifta in works of usul al-fiqh. The commentator had objected that this was inappropriate because taqlid is not part of usul. The author's response relies on two key points: one, that these topics are introduced only to clarify ijtihad (according to the principle that things are known by their opposites), and two, that the author himself presented this as a mere possibility, which is a common scholarly practice. This lesson will help you understand the methodological boundaries of usul al-fiqh and the role of contrast in exposition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Objection
Determine what the commentator (sharh) claimed against the author: that no one had taken the view that taqlid and istifta belong in usul al-fiqh, and that it is far-fetched.
The objection is based on an argument from silence.
2
Understand the Author's Defense
The author (musannif) defends by explaining that the scholars who included taqlid did not intend it as a subject but only to contrast with ijtihad, relying on the principle that things are clarified by their opposites.
Also, the author presented this as a possibility, not a strong commitment.
3
Apply the Principle of Clarification by Opposites
The principle 'tatabayyan al-ashya' bi-addadiha' is a logical tool: understanding ijtihad is deepened by understanding its opposite, taqlid. This justifies the mention without making taqlid a core subject.
This principle is widely used in usul and other Islamic sciences.
4
Evaluate the Strength of the Argument
Assess whether the commentator's objection is valid. The response shows it is not, because presenting possibilities is common, and the author's phrasing indicates he did not fully endorse the inclusion.
The author's own words 'nor is it far' (لا يبعد) signal a tentative suggestion.

کسی نے اختیار نہیں کیا اور مصنف نے جو تائید میں یہ بات پیش کی تھی کہ بعض علماء نے تقلید اور استفتاء کو اصول فقہ میں ذکر کیا ہے تو اس کا جواب یہ ہے کہ ان علماء نے خود تشریح کر دی ہے کہ تقلید کے مباحث کو اس حیثیت سے ذکر نہیں کیا جارہا کہ یہ اصول فقہ کے مباحث میں سے ہیں اور مقصود بالذکر ہیں بلکہ اس کو اجتھاد کے مقابلہ میں ذکر کیا جارہا ہے ان کے ذکر سے مقصود اجتھاد تمی وضاحت ہے کیونکہ ضابطہ ہے تتبین الاشیاء باضدادها جواب ! شارح کا مصنف پر یہ اشکال درست نہیں ہے کیونکہ مصنف نے یہ صورت بطور احتمال بیان کی ہے اور اس قسم کے احتمالات متعارف بین العلماء ہیں خود مصنف کا قول ولا یبعد مشیر ہے کہ عند المصنف یہ قول مرضی اور پسندیدہ نہیں ہے اور سابقہ عبارت ان الظاهر هذا يخص المجتهد الخ واضح ہے کہ احتمال ثانی غیر ظاہر اور بعید ہے اور شارح کا یہ قول لم یذ ہب الیہ احد بھی قابل اشکال نہیں ہے کیونکہ بہت سے لطائف ایسے ہوتے ہیں کہ ایک شخص ان کو بیان کرتا ہے اور اس سے پہلے کسی نے ان کو بیان نہیں کیا ہوتا اور ان لطائف کو کوئی شخص بھی بعید شید کہتا تو مصنف کا قول بھی اسی قبیلہ سے ہے لہذا اس کو بھی بعید کہنا درست نہیں ہے
Misk al-Malih: 4233. Sharh al-... [garbled] and someone did not choose it, and the author presented this as support that some scholars mentioned taqlid and istifta in usul al-fiqh. The response is that those scholars themselves explained that the discussions of taqlid are not being mentioned because they are from the discussions of usul al-fiqh and intended in themselves, rather they are mentioned in contrast to ijtihad. The purpose of mentioning them is to clarify ijtihad because the principle is: things are clarified by their opposites. Answer: The commentator's objection against the author is not correct because the author presented this as a possibility, and such possibilities are common among scholars. The author's own statement 'nor is it far' indicates that in his view, this statement is not approved and preferred. And the previous phrase 'apparently this is specific to the mujtahid, etc.' is clear that the second possibility is not apparent and is far. And the commentator's statement 'no one has gone to this' is also not objectionable because there are many subtle points that a person mentions and no one had mentioned them before, and if someone calls those subtle points far, then the author's statement is also of that type, therefore calling it far is not correct.
Source pages p. 235

Commentary
This passage is a usul dispute about whether the topics of taqlid and istifta belong to the science of usul al-fiqh. The author (musannif) had noted that some scholars included them, and the commentator (sharh) objected. The text here defends the author's position. First, it gives the response: those scholars did not intend to include taqlid as a subject of usul al-fiqh; they only mentioned it to clarify ijtihad, since things become clear by their opposites. The commentator's objection that no one had taken this view is also refuted: the author presented it as a possibility, and such possibilities are commonly considered by scholars. The author's own phrase 'nor is it far' shows he did not strongly endorse it. Moreover, the commentator's claim that no one held this view is not a strong objection, because an author may bring up a subtle point that no one had previously stated, and it is not necessarily far-fetched. Therefore, the commentator's criticism is not valid.

Key Terms
التقليد (at-taqlīd): Following a mujtahid without knowing his evidence.
الاستفتاء (al-istiftā'): Seeking a legal opinion from a mufti.
الاجتهاد (al-ijtihād): Independent reasoning to derive rulings from the sources.
تتبين الأشياء باضدادها (tatabayyan al-ashyā' bi-addādihā): Things are clarified by their opposites.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why did the author (musannif) defend the inclusion of taqlid in usul al-fiqh?
A) Because taqlid is a part of usul al-fiqh.
B) Because it clarifies ijtihad by contrast.
C) Because the commentator approved.
D) Because it was the established view of early scholars.

Key Takeaway
Taqlid is not a subject of usul al-fiqh; it is mentioned only to clarify ijtihad through contrast, a principle grounded in the epistemological maxim that things are clarified by their opposites.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement
advanced
10 min
Are Shar'i Evidences Muthbitat or Mu'arrafat? The Hanafi Position
Dispute on the nature of shar'i evidences

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position that shar'i evidences establish knowledge (thubut al-ilm) of rulings and evaluate Taftazani's objection that evidences are mere indicators.

Core Idea
Shar'i evidences establish knowledge of rulings; mutawatir hadith produce certainty, so denying them is kufr.

This passage addresses a foundational dispute in usul al-fiqh: do shar'i evidences actually establish rulings in reality, or do they only point to them? The author defends the Hanafi view that evidences are muthbitat of knowledge, not the ruling itself. He refutes Taftazani's objection and argues that mutawatir hadith produce certainty, as proven by the severe punishment for their denial.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two competing positions
One view: evidences are only indicators (mu'arrafat). The other: they establish rulings (muthbitat). The Hanafi school adopts the latter with a refinement.
The passage clarifies that 'establishing' means establishing knowledge (thubut al-ilm), not the ruling's essence.
2
Understand the Hanafi refinement
The author explains that even if evidences were muthbitat, they still only establish knowledge, not the ruling itself. This removes the objection about the eternity of Allah's speech.
3
Analyze Taftazani's objection
Taftazani argues that if evidences are temporal, they cannot establish an eternal ruling. He thus claims evidences are only mu'arrafat.
4
Follow the Hanafi response
The response shows that the objection is misdirected: we only seek knowledge of the ruling, not the ruling itself. The author also points to Taftazani's own inconsistency, as he previously affirmed that evidences are muthbitat.
The author also cites the custom of fuqaha who attribute rulings to evidences.
5
Examine the proof from punishment
The severe punishment (disbelief and eternal fire) for denying mutawatir hadith proves that such evidence produces certainty, not mere conjecture.
This is the key rational argument used in the passage.

Source Text

صرف معرفات اور امارات ہوتی ہیں یعنی حکم کے علم پر نشانی ہوتے ہیں ان سے واقع اور نفس الامر میں حکم ثابت نہیں ہوتا بلکہ اصل مؤثر و ثبت حکم ذات باری تعالی ہے لہذا جب ادلہ شرعیہ سے حکم ثابت ہی نہیں ہوتا تو پھر یہ تاویل کرنا کہ ثبوت سے ثبوت علم مراد ہے یا غلبہ بطن مراد ہے یہ کلام لا حاصل ہے اس سے کوئی فائدہ حاصل نہیں ہوگا۔ ولوسلم اٹھا ادلت حقیقیہ اور اگر تسلیم کر بھی لیا جائے کہ ادلہ شرعیہ معرفات امارات نہیں بلکہ مثبتات ہیں ان سے حقیقی حکم ثابت ہوتا ہے تو پھر بھی ان تاویلات کی ضرورت نہیں ہے کیونکہ دلیل کا معنی یہی ہے کہ جو کسی حکم کے ثبوت کے علم کا فائدہ دے دلیل نفس حکم کے ثبوت کا فائدہ نہیں دیتی بلکہ علم ثبوت الحکم کا فائدہ دیتی ہے اس لئے کہ یہاں بھی ادلہ علم ثبوت حکم کا فائدہ دیں گی ۔ لہذا اب نیا اشکال وارد نہیں ہوگا کہ حکم بمعنی خطاب اللہ قدیم ہے اور وہ قدیم ہونے کی وجہ سے دلیل سے ثابت نہیں ہو سکتا کیونکہ دلیل حادث ہے یہ اشکال اس لیے وارد نہیں ہوگا کہ ہم نے دلیل سے خطاب اللہ کوتو ثابت کرنا نہیں ہے بلکہ نفس خطاب اللہ تو پہلے ہی سے ثابت ہے ہم نے تو دلیل کے ساتھ صرف اس کا علم ثابت کرنا ہے اس لیے اشکال نہیں ہوسکتا زیادہ سے زیادہ ہمیں یہ تاویل کرنی پڑیگی کےعلم میں تمیم کرنی پڑے گی اس سے اور اک جازم اور رائج دونوں مراد ہوں گے تا کہ یہ علم قطعی اور نظنی دونوں کو شامل ہو جائے اور تمام ادلہ میں عام ہو جائے مقصد یہ ہوگا کہ وہ ادلہ علم ثبوت الحکم کا فائدہ دیتے ہیں خواہ علم قطعی ( ادراک جازم) کا فائدہ دیں (کمانی ادلہ ثلاثہ خواہ و علم نفی کا فائدہ دیں ( ادراک رایج ) جیسا کہ قیاس ہے، وحد الاحتفاوت بقدم الحکم وحدوثہ مقصد یہ ہے کہ دلیل کا کسی شئی کے ثبوت کے علم کا فائدہ دینا ہر حال میں ہے خواہ حکم قدیم ہو تب بھی دلیل اس کے ثبوت کے علم کا فائدہ دے گی حکم حادث ہو تب بھی دلیل سے اس کا علم ثابت ہو گا نفس حکم اس سے ثابت نہیں ہوگا اور آخر کار مصنف بھی اسی تاویل کی طرف مضطر ہو گیا اس نے بھی علم مراد لے کر اس میں تعمیم کی ہے حيث قال فتقول مزید فی الجميع اثبات العلم او غلبه النظن وليس معنى الدلیل مقصد یہ ہے کہ اگر حکم سے حکم حادث مراد لے لیں جیسا کہ مصنف کی کلام سے معلوم ہو رہا ہے کہ انہوں نے حکم کو ما ثبت بالخطاب کے معنی میں کیا ہے تب بھی دلیل اس کی ذات. کے نفس ثبوت کا فائدہ نہیں دے گی بلکہ علم ثبوت حکم کا فائدہ دے گی نفس ثبوت اور وجود خارجی صرف علل خارجیہ سے ہی ثابت ہوتا ہے مثلا وجود شمس علت ہے وجود نھار کیلئے لہذا یہ علت اور دلیل واقع اور نفس الامر میں وجود نھار کیلئے مفید ہوگی بخلاف ادلہ شرعیہ کے وہ وجود اور ثبوت کیلئے مفید نہیں ہوتی وجود حکم من جانب اللہ ہوتا ہے یہ صرف اس کیلئے علامت اور نشانی بنتی ہیں جواب ! تفتازانی صاحب نے مصنف پر جو اعتراض کیا ہے وہ غلط ہے مصنف کی کلام لا حاصل نہیں ہے بلکہ مفید ہے سب سے پہلے ہم عرض کرتے ہیں کہ تفتازانی صاحب آپ کا محبوب مشغلہ چونکہ مصنف پر اعتراض کرنا ہے اسی شوق میں آپ اپنا ماضی بھی بھول جاتے ہیں کیونکہ قبل از این خود آپ یہ عبارت تحریر کر چکے ہیں۔ ثم الثلاثة الأول اصول مطلقة لكونها ادلة مستقلة مثبتة للاحكام الخ فى تفتازاني ماذکرہ من قبل نیز ہم یہ عرض کرتے ہیں کہ تحقیق مصنف مفید ہے کیونکہ مصنف کی تحقیق دو امرین پر مبنی ہے پہلی بات تو یہ ہے احکام کی نسبت ادلہ شرعیہ کی طرف ہوتی ہے ثبوتا وانتفاء مقصد یہ ہے کہ عرف فقہاء و اصولیین میں ادلہ شرعیہ کو ثبت احکام اور نانی لا حکام کہا جاتا ہے (۲) دوسری بات یہ ہے کہ ثبوت حکم سے مراد ثبوت نفس الامری (حقیقی) اور واقعی نہیں ہے بلکہ ثبوت فی الجملہ ہے ان دو باتوں سے اتنی بات ثابت ہوگئی کہ حکم کی نسبت ہوتی ہے ادلہ کی طرف اب سوال یہ پیدا ہو حکم کا ثبوت ادلہ سے کس طریقہ پر ہوتا ہے اس کی تفصیل کی ضرورت تھی مصنف نے وہی تحقیق و تفصیل بیان فرمائی کہ اگر حکم بمعنی خطاب اللہ ہو تو ثبوت حکم من ادلہ سے مراد ثبوت علم ہوگا اور اگر حکم بمعنی ما ثبت بالخطاب ہو تو ثبوت حکم سے مراد غلبہ کن ہوگا لہذا مصنف کی یہ ساری بحث اور کلام مفید ہے نیز شارح کا یہ دعوی کہ دلائل شرعیہ صرف امارات ہیں مفید ثبوت حکم نہیں ہیں یہ غلط ہے بلکہ یہ اہل فلسفہ کا چھوڑا ہوا شوشہ ہے وہ اپنے ادلہ کو قومی کرنے اور ادلہ شرعیہ کو کمزور کرنے کیلئے کہتے ہیں کہ ہمارے اولہ تو مثبتہ ہیں لیکن ادلہ شرعیہ ادلہ حقیقیہ نہیں، ہیں اور نہ ہی وہ مفید لثبوت الحکم فی الواقع ہیں بلکہ وہ صرف ظن کا فائدہ دیتی ہیں یہ سب کچھ انہوں نے سوچی سمجھی سازش کے تحت پرو پیگنڈہ کیا تا کہ ادلہ شرعیہ کو کمزور کیا جا سکے لیکن ہمارا ایمان ہے دلائل شرعیہ قرآن وسنت خصوصاً احادیث متواترہ اور احادیث مشہورہ ادلہ حقیقیہ ہیں مثبتہ الا حکام اور مفید یقین ہیں اسی وجہ سے ان کا منکر کا فر اور مخلد فی النار ہو گا اس سے معلوم ہوا کہ ادلہ شرعیہ مفید یقین ہیں اگر ظنی ہوتیں تو اللہ تعالی ان کے انکار پر اتنی بڑی سزا تجویز نہ فرماتے لانہ عادل رحیم لا یظلم علی احدلانہ لیس بظلام العبید ۔
It appears that the entire discussion and questions and answers of the author are based on the premise that shar'i evidences are establishing rulings (muthbitat), whereas in previous pages it has been stated that shar'i evidences are not effective causes (mu'jibat) for the shar'i ruling; they are only identifiers (mu'arrafat) and indicators (amarat), i.e., they are signs for knowledge of the ruling; they do not establish the ruling in reality and in the external world; rather the actual effecter and establisher of the ruling is Allah Almighty. So when the ruling is not established by shar'i evidences at all, then interpreting 'thubut' as 'knowledge of thubut' or 'predominance of opinion' is futile and yields no benefit. And if it is conceded that shar'i evidences are not identifiers and indicators but rather establishers (muthbitat) and that from them the actual ruling is established, then also these interpretations are unnecessary because the meaning of 'dalil' is that which gives benefit of knowledge of the ruling's establishment, not the establishment of the ruling itself; rather it gives benefit of knowledge of the ruling's establishment. Therefore, no new objection arises that the ruling in the sense of the speech of Allah (khitab Allah) is eternal and cannot be established by a proof because the proof is temporal. This objection does not arise because we do not intend to establish the speech of Allah by the proof; rather the speech of Allah is already established; we only intend to establish knowledge of it by the proof. So no objection is possible. At most we would need to interpret the word 'ilm' to include both certain (jazim) and prevalent (ra'ij) knowledge so that it includes both certain and speculative knowledge and encompasses all evidences. The purpose is that those evidences give benefit of knowledge of the ruling's establishment, whether they give benefit of certain knowledge (as in the three [certain] evidences) or give benefit of speculative knowledge (as in qiyas). And there is no difference whether the ruling is eternal or temporal. The purpose is that the proof giving benefit of knowledge of the establishment of something is in all cases; whether the ruling is eternal, the proof gives benefit of knowledge of its establishment; whether the ruling is temporal, knowledge of it is established by the proof, but the ruling itself is not established by it. Eventually, the author was also compelled towards this interpretation; he also took 'ilm' and generalized it, saying: 'Thus you say: Additionally in all of them it is the establishment of knowledge or the preponderance of conjecture, and this is the meaning of dalil.' The purpose is that if by 'hukm' one means the temporal ruling, as is apparent from the author's speech that he made 'hukm' in the sense of 'ma thabata bil-khitab', then also the proof will not give benefit of the essential establishment of the ruling itself; rather it will give benefit of knowledge of the ruling's establishment. The essential existence and external existence are only established by external causes; for example, the existence of the sun is the cause for the existence of the day. So this cause/proof will benefit the existence of the day in reality and the external world. Unlike shar'i evidences, they do not benefit existence and establishment; the existence of the ruling is from Allah; they are only signs and indicators for it. Answer: The objection of Taftazani against the author is wrong; the author's speech is not futile but beneficial. First, we say: O Taftazani, since your favorite pastime is to object to the author, in this enthusiasm you even forget your own past because previously you yourself had written this statement: 'The first three [evidences] are absolute principles because they are independent proofs that establish rulings' — look at Taftazani's earlier mention. Also we say that the author's research is beneficial because it is based on two matters: First, the attribution of rulings to shar'i evidences occurs in terms of establishment and negation; meaning in the custom of fuqaha and usuliyyun, shar'i evidences are called 'muthbitat al-ahkam' (establishing rulings) and 'nafi al-ahkam' (negating rulings). Second, by 'thubut al-hukm' is not meant the real, essential establishment but 'thubut fi al-jumla' (general establishment). From these two matters it is established that the ruling is attributed to the evidences. Now the question arises: In what manner does the establishment of the ruling occur from the evidences? The author provided that research and detail: If 'hukm' means the speech of Allah, then 'thubut al-hukm min al-adillah' refers to the establishment of knowledge; if 'hukm' means 'ma thabata bil-khitab', then 'thubut al-hukm' refers to preponderance of opinion. Therefore, all of the author's discussion and speech is beneficial. Furthermore, the commentator's claim that shar'i evidences are only indicators and do not benefit the establishment of rulings is false; rather, it is a trick left by the philosophers. To strengthen their own proofs and weaken shar'i evidences, they say: 'Our proofs are muthbitat, but shar'i evidences are not real proofs nor do they benefit the establishment of rulings in reality; they only benefit conjecture.' All this is propaganda under a calculated conspiracy to weaken shar'i evidences. But our belief is that shar'i evidences—Qur'an and Sunnah, especially mutawatir and mashhur hadiths—are real proofs, muthbitat of rulings, and beneficial for certainty. For this reason, the denier of them is a disbeliever and will be eternally in the Fire. From this it is known that shar'i evidences are beneficial for certainty; if they were speculative, Allah would not have prescribed such a severe punishment for denying them, for He is just, merciful, does not wrong anyone, and is not unjust to His servants.
Source pages p. 245

Commentary
This passage is a dispute about the nature of shar'i evidences. The author defends the position that shar'i evidences are not mere indicators but actually establish rulings, specifically in terms of knowledge. The text begins by summarizing the objection: some claim that evidences are only signs (mu'arrafat) and do not cause the ruling itself; the author argues that even if they were muthbitat, they still establish knowledge, not the ruling's essence. The discussion addresses the objection about the eternity of Allah's speech and the temporality of proofs. The author, following Sadr al-Shari'ah, concludes that 'thubut' should be interpreted as 'thubut al-ilm' (establishment of knowledge) universally. The passage then directly refutes Taftazani's criticism, pointing out his inconsistency and reaffirming that shar'i evidences, especially mutawatir hadith, are a source of certainty, as evidenced by the severe punishment for their deniers. The commentary emphasizes that the Hanafi position is that adillah are muthbitat of certainty, not mere speculation.

Key Terms
دلیل شرعی (dalil shar'i): A shar'i evidence that provides knowledge or indication of a shar'i ruling.
معرفات (mu'arrafat): Identifiers; evidences that do not create the ruling but point to it.
مثبتات (muthbitat): Establishing proofs; evidences that cause the ruling to be established in knowledge or reality.
ثبوت علم (thubut al-ilm): The establishment of knowledge regarding a ruling's existence or non-existence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the author argue that shar'i evidences, particularly mutawatir hadith, produce certainty (yaqeen)?
A) Because they are based on rational proofs.
B) Because the denier of mutawatir hadith is declared a disbeliever and punished with eternal fire.
C) Because all scholars agree on their authenticity.
D) Because they are mentioned in the Quran.

Key Takeaway
Shar'i evidences, especially mutawatir hadith, produce certain knowledge; they are muthbitat of knowledge, not the ruling itself, and their denial leads to kufr.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement
