لگائی ہے کہ بھول کر غیر عربی میں قراءت کرے اگر عمدا کی ہے تو اس کو یا تو مجنون قرار دیکر اس کا علاج کیا جائے گا یا اس کو زندیق قرار دے کر قتل کر دیا جائے گا۔ واما الكلام فى ان ركن الشئى كيف لا يكون لازما فسیجی ، مقصد یہ ہے کہ امام صاحب نے نظم کو رکن غیر لازم کیسے قرار دیا جب کہ رکن شئی کاشی کو لازم ہوتا ہے تو شارح کہ رہے ہیں اس کی وضاحت عنقریب آجائے گی۔ فان قيل ان كان المعنى قرآنا فيلزم عدم اعتبار النظم فى القرآن غرض شارح جواب سوال۔ سوال ! یہ ہوتا ہے کہ امام صاحب کے نزدیک قرآن مجید فقط معنی کا نام ہے یا نظم وال علی المعنی کا نام ہے اول شق پر دو اشکال لازم آتے ہیں (۱) پہلا اشکال یہ لازم آتا ہے کہ اگر قرآن پاک فقط معنی کا نام ہو تو الفاظ قرآن قرآن سے خارج ہو جائیں گے حالانکہ یہ محال ہے کیونکہ الفاظ یا تو عین قرآن میں ( کما ھوا تحقیق ) یا ۔ جزء قرآن نہیں ( علی التسامج ) (۲) دوسرا اشکال یہ لازم آئے گا قرآن پاک کی تعریف ( المنزل على الرسول المنقول عنہ المكتوب في المصاحف ) قرآن مجید پر صادق نہیں آئے گی کیونکہ کتابت نقل - تنزیل فقط معنی کی صفات نہیں بن سکتی بلکہ یہ نظم کی صفات ہیں لہذا حد صادق نہیں آئے گی حالانکہ اس حد کو جامع مانع قرار دیا گیا ہے (۲) اگر شق بثانی اختیار کی جائے اور قرآن نظم دال علی المعنی قرار دیا جائے تو پھر یہ اشکال لازم آئے گا کہ عندالامام نماز میں قرآن مجید کی قراءت فرض نہیں ہے کیونکہ جب قرآن مجید نام ہے نظم کا اور امام صاحب نے نظم کو رکن لازم فی الصلوۃ قرار نہیں دیا بلکہ صرف معنی کی قراءت کو جائز
Commentary on al-Tawdih wa al-Talwih. Misk al-Milh: In linguistic meaning it is rudeness because its meaning is 'to throw from the mouth'. So the problem is repelled. He said: 'Rather, the meaning is considered because the structure is based on expansion and the meaning is the intended, especially in the state of whispered prayer.' Purpose of the text: All imams agree that if a person cannot recite in Arabic, then non-Arabic recitation in prayer is permissible. But for one who is capable of Arabic, there is a dispute. The three imams (Abu Yusuf, Muhammad, al-Shafi'i) and the Sahibayn say he cannot recite in non-Arabic. Their proof: Recitation of Quran in prayer is a pillar and obligation. Allah says: 'So recite what is easy of the Quran.' And 'Quran' is the name of Arabic composition, as proven by texts: 'We have sent it down as an Arabic Quran' etc. So it is clear that the Arabic wording is part of the Quran; in prayer, recitation in Arabic is necessary. Imam Abu Hanifa's opinion: If someone capable of Arabic recites in his own language without excuse, only conveying the meaning without the words, his prayer is valid and the pillar of recitation is fulfilled. So Imam Abu Hanifa does not consider the composition as a necessary pillar for the validity of prayer. Proof (1): Because the wording is based on expansion, meaning ease and facility, since words themselves are not the goal, the real goal is the meaning. Words are a means. The Quran was first revealed in the dialect of Quraysh, the most eloquent, but other tribes found it difficult. The Prophet prayed to Allah for ease, and Allah allowed each tribe to recite in its own dialect, and even allowed a Qurayshi to leave his own dialect and recite in another. So if a Qurayshi, who is capable of his own dialect and eloquent, can leave it and recite in another dialect, then a non-Arab who is not fully capable of Arabic should be permitted even more so to leave Arabic words and recite in his own language. Therefore, Imam Abu Hanifa allowed non-Arabic recitation and did not consider Arabic composition a necessary pillar. Since this concession is a dropping of requirement, it is permissible whether there is an excuse or not, just as the prayer during travel is a concession dropping requirement. Proof (2): Ease in recitation is considered; the follower's recitation is dropped. Allah says: 'Recite what is easy of the Quran.' Sometimes a person is constrained in Arabic recitation, so for ease, the Arabic composition is not required. Proof (3): The author of Nur al-Anwar stated that Imam Azam was immersed in the sea of tawhid; his view was that the state of prayer is a state of whispered conversation with Allah; attention should only be to Allah. If Arabic composition were a necessary pillar, the worshipper's attention would shift to the miraculous words and arrangement, losing the main purpose. So he did not require the words. Proof (4): From the verse 'and indeed it is in the scriptures of the former' (al-Zukhruf?), the pronoun refers to the Quran, though earlier scriptures were not in Arabic; they were called Quran, showing that the essential element is only the meaning. And that is not accused of any innovation. Here the commentator Taftazani specifies the points of difference: (1) According to Imam Abu Hanifa, non-Arabic recitation is permissible for one who is not a bid'ati; if he is a bid'ati, it is not permissible. (2) This dispute is about someone who is reciting in Arabic and then substitutes a word or two with non-Arabic; that is permissible. If he recites entirely in non-Arabic, it is not permissible. (3) The condition that the non-Arabic word not have multiple meanings or possibility of erroneous interpretation. (4) And it was said: without excessive change? Some said he should only translate the literal meaning; if he makes excessive changes, the prayer is invalid. (5) And it was said: without deliberately reciting in non-Arabic; if done deliberately, he is either considered mad and treated, or considered a zindiq and executed. And the discussion about how the pillar of a thing can be non-necessary will come later. If it is said: If the meaning is the Quran, then it necessitates that the composition is not considered in the Quran. Purpose of the commentator: Answer to the question. The question: According to Imam Abu Hanifa, is the Quran only meaning or composition upon meaning? The first alternative leads to two problems: (1) If Quran is only meaning, then words would be outside the Quran, which is impossible because words are either the very Quran (as is the correct view) or not part of the Quran (according to the lax view). (2) The definition of Quran (revealed to the Messenger, transmitted from him, written in the musahif) would not apply to the Quran because writing, transmission, and revelation are qualities of the composition, not of mere meaning. So the definition would not be inclusive and exclusive. If the second alternative is adopted, that Quran is composition indicative of meaning, then the problem is that according to the Imam, recitation of the Quran in prayer is not obligatory because when Quran is the name of composition and the Imam does not consider composition a necessary pillar, rather he allows only the recitation of meaning.
Source pages p. 296

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a dispute in usul al-fiqh regarding the permissibility of reciting non-Arabic (specifically Persian) in prayer for a person who is capable of reciting in Arabic. The text first establishes the agreed-upon point: if a person cannot recite Arabic, non-Arabic recitation is permissible by consensus. The dispute arises when the person is capable of Arabic. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) and the three imams (including al-Shafi'i) hold that non-Arabic recitation is not allowed. Their proof is that the Quran is defined as Arabic composition; the verse 'faqra'u ma tayassara min al-qur'an' commands recitation of the Quran, which is Arabic. Therefore, Arabic wording is a pillar of prayer. Imam Abu Hanifa, however, holds that the prayer is valid even if one recites the meaning in Persian, without the Arabic wording. He provides four proofs: (1) The Quran was revealed with ease; the Prophet allowed different Arabic dialects, and analogically, non-Arabic is also permissible, especially for non-Arabs. (2) The principle of ease in prayer, exemplified by the follower not reciting. (3) The purpose of prayer is devotion to Allah; focusing on miraculous wording distracts from this purpose. (4) The term 'Quran' applied to earlier scriptures in non-Arabic, proving that the essence is the meaning, not the wording. The commentator then clarifies that this permissibility is conditioned: the person must not be a bid'ati (innovator), only partial substitution is allowed (a few words), the translation must be univocal, and some require it to be unintentional. Finally, a question is raised: if the meaning alone constitutes the Quran, then the definition of Quran fails; if the composition is included, then Abu Hanifa's position seems to negate the necessity of reciting Quran in prayer. The answer is postponed.

Key Terms
نظم (nazm): The Arabic composition and wording of the Quran.
معنى (ma'na): The meaning or content of the Quran, as opposed to its wording.
ركن (rukn): A pillar or essential part of an act of worship without which the act is invalid.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position presented in this lesson, what is the main usul basis for allowing Persian recitation in prayer for one capable of Arabic?
A) The verse 'faqra'u ma tayassara min al-qur'an' is khass and applies only to Arabic.
B) The term 'al-qur'an' in the verse is amm, and through tafsir and qiyas it can include meaning.
C) The prayer is not considered valid unless the exact Arabic wording is used.
D) The Prophet explicitly allowed Persian recitation for all Muslims.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi position on recitation in prayer prioritizes meaning over wording, based on usul principles of amm, tafsir, and qiyas, but is conditioned by later scholars to maintain the sanctity of the Quran.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement
advanced
9 min
Debate on Wording vs Meaning in Qur'an Recitation
Defending Abu Hanifa's position that meaning is the essential rukn of Qur'an

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the partitive 'min' (min tab'idiyya) in Qur'anic texts and apply the Hanafi usul principle of ba'd tarkibi vs ba'd basiti to derive the ruling on sufficiency of meaning in prayer.

Core Idea
The partitive 'min' in 'min al-Qur'an' allows reciting either wording or meaning, making meaning alone sufficient in prayer.

This lesson unpacks a classic usul dispute: Is the Qur'an essentially its meaning (ma'na) or its wording (nazm) indicating meaning? At stake is the validity of reciting Persian in prayer. We will trace Imam Abu Hanifa's dalil from the Qur'an, analyze the partitive 'min', and see how his position is defended against objections. The key is understanding how a rukn (pillar) can be non-lazim (non-essential) in form.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core dispute
State the two positions: Is the Qur'an only meaning, or wording indicating meaning? Recognize that the Hanafi school holds meaning as the essential rukn of prayer recitation.
This dispute affects permissibility of non-Arabic recitation.
2
Examine the first dalil naqli
Look at the verse 'wa innahu lafi zuburi al-awwalin' (26:196) where the pronoun refers to the Qur'an, yet earlier scriptures were in non-Arabic. This suggests the Qur'an's essence is meaning, not wording.
Al-Marghinani uses this in Al-Hidayah.
3
Analyze the primary dalil: partitive 'min'
Focus on 'faqra'u ma tayassara min al-Qur'an' (73:20). The 'min' is partitive (min tab'idiyya). Understand the two types of 'ba'd' (part): ba'd tarkibi (a portion of the verses) and ba'd basiti (a portion of the constituents—wording or meaning). The partitive includes both, so meaning alone suffices.
Hanafi usul treats this as a valid takhsis.
4
Work through the objections and responses
Two objections are raised: (1) if only meaning is Qur'an, words are excluded; (2) the definition of Qur'an fails. The response uses 'taqdir' (estimation): Persian is considered a substitute (qa'im maqam) of Arabic wording, preserving the definition. Alternatively, if wording is included, the obligation is fulfilled by meaning through the partitive 'min'.
The commentator Taftazani refines the scope.
5
Conclude with thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference (thamarah) is that Hanafis allow Persian recitation for those unable to read Arabic, while the opposing school restricts it to Arabic. Also note the conditions: not for heretics, only a few non-Arabic words, and no excessive alteration.
This illustrates istihsan for facilitation.

لو اور بعض کی دو صورتیں ہیں (۱) ایک یہ ہے کہ مجموعہ قرآن میں سے بعض پڑھ لو مثلا ایک آیت یا دو آیت اس کو بعض ترکیبی کہتے ہیں (۲) دوسری صورت یہ ہے کہ قرآن مجید جو کہ مجموعہ نظم اور معنی کا نام ہے تم ان میں سے بعض پڑھ لو یعنی صرف نظم پڑھ لو یا صرف معنی پڑھ لو اس کو بعض بسیطی کہتے ہیں اور من تبعیضیہ دونوں بعضوں کو شامل ہے اس لیے ثابت ہوا کہ صرف معنی پر اکتفا کر لینا جائز ہے! ( انظر حاشیہ نمبر ۳ صفحہ ۷۹) فان قيل فعلى الاول يلزم الجمع بين الحقيقة والمجاز غرض جواب سوال سوال ایہ ہوتا ہے کہ قرآن پاک کا اطلاق منظم عربی پر حقیقت ہے غیر عربی پر مجاز ہے آپ نے فاقرء واما تیسر من القرآن میں نظم عربی اور نظم غیر عربی دونوں مراد لے لیے ہیں یہ تو جمع بین الحقیقت والمجاز ہے جو کہ عند الاحناف باطل ہے. قلنا سے جواب دے رہے ہیں کہ یہاں کوئی جمع بین الحقیقت والمجاز نہیں ہے بلکہ نص سے صرف حقیقی معنی قرآن عربی مراد ہے باقی غیر عربی کوی تو نظم عربی پر قیاس کر کے جواز صلوۃ ثابت
This is a commentary on al-Tawdih wa al-Talwih. It says: If Arabic wording (nazm) in prayer is made an essential pillar (rukn lazım), then since Arabic wording is miraculous, the worshipper's attention would turn away from Allah to the words and composition of the Qur'an, defeating the main purpose of prayer. Therefore Imam [Abu Hanifa] did not make wording necessary but allowed relying on meaning as the essential pillar. (4) Evidence: And He says 'it' in the verse 'wa innahu lafi zuburi al-awwalin' — Imam holds that the pronoun refers to the Qur'an, yet in earlier scriptures it was not in Arabic wording but in non-Arabic, yet it was called Qur'an. Thus it is known that the essential pillar in the Qur'an is only meaning. 'And that is not accused of any innovation' — here commentator Taftazani determines the scope of the dispute: According to Imam, recitation in non-Arabic is permissible for a person who is not a heretic (bid'ati); if he is a heretic, it is not permissible. (2) Similarly, this dispute concerns someone who is reciting in Arabic and during recitation pronounces one or two words in non-Arabic — it is permissible; if he recites the entire recitation in non-Arabic, it is not permissible. (3) Similarly, it is a condition that in non-Arabic he utters a word which is not ambiguous in meaning and does not admit of interpretation (ta'wil). (4) 'And it is said without disturbing the greatness' — some have also conditioned that the Arabic words should only be given their lexical meaning; if he makes excessive changes, the prayer becomes invalid. (5) 'And it is said without intention' — some have conditioned that he recites in non-Arabi by mistake; if intentionally, then either he is considered insane and treated, or considered a heretic and killed. 'And the discussion about why a pillar is not necessary will come soon' — the point is: how did Imam make wording a non-essential pillar when a pillar is inherently necessary? The commentator says its explanation will come soon. 'If it is said: If meaning is the Qur'an, then the disregard of wording in the Qur'an follows' — the purpose of the commentator is to answer the question. The question: According to Imam, is the Noble Qur'an only meaning (ma'na) or wording (nazm) indicating meaning? For the first branch, two objections follow: (1) First objection: If the Qur'an is only meaning, then the words would be excluded from the Qur'an, which is impossible because words are either exactly the Qur'an or part of it. (2) Second objection: The definition of the Qur'an (revealed to the Messenger, transmitted from him, written in the codices) would not apply, because writing, transmission, revelation are not attributes of meaning alone but of wording; so the definition would not hold, even though it is considered comprehensive and exclusive. (2) If the second branch is adopted and the Qur'an is considered wording indicating meaning, then this objection follows: according to Imam, recitation of the Qur'an in prayer is not obligatory, because when the Qur'an is the name of wording and Imam did not make wording a necessary pillar but allowed relying on meaning — which is not the Qur'an — so it would mean that Imam holds recitation of the Qur'an in prayer is not obligatory. This is also invalid because it contradicts the explicit text of His statement: 'faqra'u ma tayassara min al-Qur'an' — we say: He established the Persian expression [as a substitute]. The commentator gives two answers to this objection: (1) First answer: We adopt the first branch, that the Qur'an is only meaning. There were two objections: (i) disregard of wording in the Qur'an, (ii) the definition not applying. The answer to both is that these objections would be valid if Imam had declared wording absolutely irrelevant — neither Arabic wording nor its substitute; but this is incorrect. Rather, Imam considers Persian as standing in place of Arabic wording and calls it 'taqdir Qur'an' (estimated as Qur'an). Thus both objections are removed: Imam has given consideration to wording, and the definition also applies to non-Arabic Persian etc., because although Persian is not actually revealed, written, and transmitted, it is estimated as written and transmitted between the covers of the codices. Some have said this is the answer for the second branch — that the Qur'an is not only meaning but wording is also necessary; then the objection was that the non-obligation of reciting the Qur'an would follow. The answer: The objection of non-obligation does not follow because Imam considers Persian wording as a substitute for Arabic wording; hence the obligation of reciting the Qur'an is fulfilled with this Persian wording. 'First His statement' — from here the second answer: We adopt the second branch, that the Noble Qur'an is wording indicating meaning. Then the objection was that according to Imam the non-obligation of reciting the Qur'an in prayer would follow. The answer: Imam took the verse 'faqra'u ma tayassara min al-Qur'an' to mean only the meaning of the Qur'an, not the wording; therefore he said: only regard for meaning is obligatory in prayer, not the word. And Imam had evidence for this: the 'min' in 'min al-Qur'an' is partitive, so the meaning is: recite some of the Qur'an. And 'some' has two forms: (1) some of the whole Qur'an, e.g., one or two verses — this is called 'ba'd tarkibi' (compositional part); (2) some of the Qur'an, which is a composition of wording and meaning — you recite some of them, i.e., only wording or only meaning — this is called 'ba'd basiti' (simple part). The partitive 'min' includes both kinds, so it is proven that relying on meaning alone is permissible. (See footnote 3 page 79). 'If it is said: According to the first, combining literal and metaphorical usage is necessary' — the purpose is to answer the question. The question: The application of 'Qur'an' to Arabic wording is literal, to non-Arabic is metaphorical. You took in 'faqra'u ma tayassara min al-Qur'an' both Arabic wording and non-Arabic wording — this is combining literal and metaphorical, which according to the Hanafis is invalid. The answer: There is no combination of literal and metaphorical here; rather, from the text only the literal meaning of Arabic Qur'an is intended; as for non-Arabic, the permissibility of prayer is established by analogy (qiyas) upon Arabic wording.
Source pages p. 297

Commentary
This passage is a defense of Imam Abu Hanifa's position regarding the essence of the Qur'an in prayer. The debate centers on whether the Qur'an is defined solely by its meaning (ma'na) or by its wording (nazm) together with meaning. Imam Abu Hanifa takes the view that the essential pillar (rukn) in prayer is the meaning, not the specific Arabic wording. This is because the purpose of prayer is devotion to Allah, and fixation on the miraculous nature of Arabic wording can distract from that purpose. He allows recitation in Persian (non-Arabic) for those who cannot read Arabic, treating it as a valid substitute (qa'im maqam) that fulfills the obligation. The opponents raise two main objections: first, if only meaning is Qur'an, then the wording is excluded, contradicting the definition of the Qur'an as revealed, written, and transmitted; second, if the Qur'an includes both, then since Imam does not require wording, reciting meaning alone would not fulfill the obligation. The commentator, Taftazani, provides two answers: either the first branch (meaning only) is adopted, but then Persian is considered an estimated substitute (taqdir), thus preserving the definition; or the second branch (wording indicating meaning) is adopted, but then the verse 'faqra'u ma tayassara min al-Qur'an' is interpreted with the partitive 'min' to allow reciting either the wording part or the meaning part. The concern about combining literal and metaphorical usage is dismissed because the literal sense applies to Arabic, and Persian is justified by analogy (qiyas) for ease. The passage also specifies conditions for permissible non-Arabic recitation: the person must not be a heretic, must recite only a few words, and the words must be unambiguous and not involve excessive alteration. This reflects the Hanafi principle of istihsan (juristic preference) for facilitation.

Key Terms
نظم (nazm): The wording or composition of the Qur'an; the Arabic text.
معنى (ma'na): The meaning or content of the Qur'an; the intended message.
ركن (rukn): An essential component or pillar of an act; here, of prayer.
من تبعيضية (min tab'idiyya): The partitive preposition 'min' indicating a portion of the whole.
بعض ترکیبی (ba'd tarkibi): A compositional part; a portion of the verses of the Qur'an.
بعض بسیطی (ba'd basiti): A simple part; one of the essential constituents (wording or meaning) of the Qur'an.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi usul argument presented, why does the partitive 'min' in 'min al-Qur'an' allow reciting meaning alone?
A) Because it specifies that only a portion of the Qur'an is required, and meaning is a portion of its essence
B) Because the Qur'an is defined as meaning, not wording
C) Because the verse was abrogated
D) Because 'min' here means 'from' indicating source, not partition

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school uses the partitive 'min' and the concept of taqdir to allow recitation of meaning alone in prayer, prioritizing facilitation without abandoning the Qur'anic obligation.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement
advanced
10 min
Quran Recitation in Non-Arabic Languages: Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on recitation language

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the evidence used by later Hanafis to restrict Quran recitation to Arabic and evaluate the Hanafi response (tarjih).

Core Idea
Imam Abu Hanifa initially allowed Quran in any language; later Hanafis restricted it due to the Quran's description as Arabic tongue.

This passage from Taftazani's commentary on al-Tawdih explores a classic Hanafi dispute: Can you recite the Quran in Persian (or any non-Arabic language) during prayer? Imam Abu Hanifa famously said yes, but later Hanafis disagreed, citing the Quran's own description of itself as Arabic. The passage also reveals that Imam Abu Hanifa eventually retracted his view. Understanding this dispute teaches us how later Hanafis (mutakhkhirun) applied precaution (ihtiyat) and how the school's operative position evolved.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions
First, note Imam Abu Hanifa's initial ruling: recitation in any language is valid in prayer. Then note the second opinion (qila) that only Persian is permissible because of its proximity to Arabic. Finally, the later Hanafis (mutakhkhirun) extended this to require sujood for Persian and prohibit touching a Persian mus-haf.
These positions represent a spectrum from permissive to restrictive.
2
Analyze the evidence (dalil) for each view
Imam Abu Hanifa's proof: the meaning (ma'na) is the essence; the Arabic wording (nazm arabi) is not essential. The later Hanafis' proof: the Quran describes itself as 'Arabic tongue' (lisān 'arabī) in multiple verses, making Arabic an essential attribute.
The Quranic verses are explicit: e.g., Surah Yusuf 12:2, Surah az-Zukhruf 43:3.
3
Trace the reasoning of the later Hanafis
They observed that the essential intended pillar (rukn maqsudi asli) in prayer is the meaning, but they also saw that the Quranic text emphasizes Arabic. To reconcile, they adopted a precautionary approach: requiring sujood for Persian recitation and prohibiting touching a Persian translation, without declaring prayer invalid if done in Persian.
This is a case of ihtiyat (precaution) overriding the original rukhsa (concession).
4
Understand Imam Abu Hanifa's retraction
According to Nuh ibn Abi Maryam's narration, Imam Abu Hanifa retracted his initial view because it apparently contradicted the Quranic description of itself as Arabic. Fakhr al-Islam explains that despite Abu Hanifa's own proofs, the external evidence from the Book of Allah compelled him to align with Abu Yusuf and Muhammad.
This retraction is considered 'the correct view' (asahh) in the text.
5
Identify the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
If someone recites the Quran in Persian during prayer: according to the early view, prayer is valid; according to the later (and retracted) view, prayer is invalid because recitation must be in Arabic. For sujood and touching, the later Hanafis still apply their precautionary rulings.
The operative position for fatwa today is the later view based on the Quranic evidence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله بغير العربية اشارة الى ان الفارسية وغيرها الخ غرض تفتازانى توضیح متن ! کہ مصنف نے ب غیر العربیہ کا لفظ ذکر کیا بالفارسیۃ کا لفظ ذکر نہیں کیا اشارہ کر دیا کہ فارسی غیر فارسی اردو سرائیکی، پشتو، سب اس میں داخل ہیں کسی لغت میں قراءت کر لی امام صاحب کے نزدیک نماز ہو جائے گی. وقیل سے دوسرا قول ذکر کر رہے ہیں عند البعض غیر عربی ہے صرف فارسی مراد ہے صرف فارسی میں قراءت جائز ہے پشتو ، پنجابی اردو وغیرہ میں جائز نہیں ہے کیونکہ فارسی عربی کے قریب ہے اہل جنت کی لسان جس طرح عربی ہوگی فارسی بھی ہوگی ( حاشیہ ۷ صفحه ۸) حتى لو قرء اية اشارة الى انه لا يجوز الاعتياد والمداومة الخ غرض تو صحیح متن شارح فرماتے ہیں مصنف نے قر، ماضی کا صیغہ اور ایته نکرہ لا کر اشارہ کر دیا کہ جنبی اور حائضہ کیلئے بلکہ پاک آدمی کیلئے بھی قرآن مجید کی قراءت بالغاز سیہ کی عادت بنالینا اور اس پر دوام کرنا جائز نہیں ہے بلکہ حرام ہے کیونکہ یہ قرآن پاک کی عظمت و تعظیم کے خلاف ہے۔ فان قيل المتاخرون على انه الخ غرض جواب سوال سوال ! متن کے لفظ خاصتہ پر وارد ہوتا ہے، سوال یہ ہے کہ متاخرین حنفیه علامه شیخ الاسلام خواهر زادہ وغیرہ یہ کہتے ہیں کہ اگر کسی نے آیت سجدہ فارسی میں تلاوت کر لی اس پر سجدہ تلاوت واجب ہو جاتا ہے (۲) اگر فارسی زبان میں قرآن مجید لکھا ہوا ہے تو متاخرین کے نزدیک ناپاک آدمی کیلئے اس کو ہاتھ لگانا جائز نہیں ہے تو ان دو مسئلوں سے ثابت ہوا کہ نماز کے علاوہ دوسری جگہوں پر بھی امام صاحب نے نظم عربی کو جزء قرآن اور لازم قرار نہیں دیا لہذا خاصہ کا لفظ ذکر کرنا باطل ہے. قلنا سے جواب دیا ہے کہ مصنف نے خاصہ کا لفظ متقدمین کے مذہب کے مطابق ذکر کیا ہے کیونکہ ان سے ان مسائل میں کوئی روایت منصوصہ نہیں ہے اور متاخرین حضرات نے ان مسائل میں جو مذہب اختیار کیا ہے وہ بھی مبنی بر احتیاط ہے کیونکہ انہوں نے دیکھا کہ رکن مقصودی اصلی یعنی معنی موجود و تحقیق ہے اس لیے انہوں نے احتیاطا وجوب سجدہ اور حرمت میں مصحف کا قول کیا ۔ قوله لكن الاصح انه رجع الى قولهما الخ غرض توضیح متن اکہ امام صاحب نے اپنے اس قول سے رجوع فرمالیا جیسا کہ نوح بن ابی مریم امام صاحب سے روایت کرتے ہیں علامہ فخر الاسلام نے رجوع کی وجہ بیان کرتے ہوئے کہا کہ اگر چہ امام صاحب کے اپنے دلائل ہیں لیکن بظاہر امام صاحب کا یہ قول کتاب اللہ کے خلاف ہے کیونکہ قرآن مجید میں دو تین مقامات پر قرآن پاک کو لسان عربی کے ساتھ موصوف کیا گیا ہے وقال ابو الیسر فخر الاسلام کے بھائی علامہ ابو الیسر فرماتے ہیں یہ مسئلہ انتہائی مشکل ہے کیونکہ کسی کیلئے بھی امام صاحب کا قول واضح نہیں ہو سکا کہ امام صاحب کی کیا مراد ہے اور علامہ کرتی نے اس مسئلہ میں ایک طویل تصنیف کی ہے لیکن
His statement: 'with other than Arabic' indicates that Persian and other [languages]... The purpose of Taftazani in the explanation of the text: The author mentioned the word 'bi ghayr al-arabiyya' and did not mention 'bi al-farisiyya', indicating that Persian, non-Persian, Urdu, Saraiki, Pashto, all are included. If one recites in any language, according to Imam Abu Hanifa, the prayer is valid. And the second opinion (qila) is that 'other than Arabic' only means Persian; recitation is permissible only in Persian, not in Pashto, Punjabi, Urdu, etc., because Persian is close to Arabic, and the language of the people of Paradise will be Arabic and also Persian. (Footnote 7, page 8) Even if one recites a verse [in Persian], it indicates that it is not permissible to make a habit and persistence [in such recitation]... the correct explanation: the commentator says the author used the past tense verb 'qaraa' and the indefinite 'aya' to indicate that for a junub or a menstruating woman, and also for a pure person, it is not permissible to make a habit of reciting the Quran in a playful manner and to persist in it; rather it is forbidden because it is against the majesty and reverence of the Quran. If it is said: The later scholars (mutakhkhirun) such as Allamah Shaykh al-Islam Khwahar Zada etc. say that if someone recites a verse of prostration in Persian, the prostration of recitation becomes obligatory (2) and if the Quran is written in Persian, according to them, an impure person may not touch it. So from these two issues it is proven that outside of prayer also, Imam Abu Hanifa did not consider the Arabic wording as part of the Quran and necessary; therefore mentioning the word 'particular' (khassa) is invalid. The answer: The author mentioned the word 'khassa' according to the madhhab of the early scholars (mutaqaddimin) because there is no transmitted narration from them on these issues. And the later scholars (mutakhkhirin) adopted a madhhab based on precaution because they saw that the essential intended pillar (rukn maqsudi asli) is the meaning that exists and is verified; therefore they precautionarily obliged prostration and prohibited touching the mus-haf. His statement: 'But the correct view is that he [Abu Hanifa] retracted to the opinion of the two [Abu Yusuf and Muhammad]'... The purpose of the explanation: Imam Abu Hanifa retracted from his opinion, as narrated by Nuh ibn Abi Maryam from him. Fakhr al-Islam explained the reason for retraction: although Imam Abu Hanifa had his own proofs, apparently his statement is against the Book of Allah because in two or three places in the Quran, the Quran is described as 'Arabic tongue'. And Abu al-Yusr, the brother of Fakhr al-Islam, said: This issue is extremely difficult because no one has been able to clarify what Imam Abu Hanifa meant. And Allamah Karti wrote a long treatise on this issue, but [he did not provide sufficient proof].
Source pages p. 299

Commentary
This passage is a commentary by Taftazani on the text of al-Tawdih, discussing the ruling on reciting the Quran in languages other than Arabic. The author of the matn used the phrase 'bi ghayr al-arabiyya' (other than Arabic) rather than 'bi al-farisiyya' (in Persian), which implies that all non-Arabic languages are included, not just Persian. According to Imam Abu Hanifa, reciting the Quran in any language renders the prayer valid. However, there is a second view that only Persian is permissible because of its proximity to Arabic. The commentator then clarifies that making a habit of reciting in a playful manner is forbidden. The later Hanafis (mutakhkhirun), such as Khwahar Zada, ruled that if one recites a verse of prostration in Persian, the prostration of recitation is obligatory, and an impure person may not touch a Quran written in Persian. This seems to contradict the view that the Arabic wording is not essential. The response is that the author of the matn followed the early scholars (mutaqaddimin), while the later scholars based their view on precaution. Finally, it is noted that Imam Abu Hanifa himself retracted from his opinion because the Quran describes itself as Arabic. The discussion highlights the complexity of the issue and disagreement within the Hanafi school.

Key Terms
نظم عربي (nazm arabi): The Arabic wording of the Quran as revealed, considered by some as an essential part of the Quran.
خاصة (khassa): Particular; in this context, it refers to the specific exception that the Arabic wording is essential for prayer.
متاخرون (mutakhkhirun): Later Hanafi scholars who adopted a precautionary approach in this issue.
لسان عربي (lisan arabi): Arabic tongue; the Quran describes itself with this phrase.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what was the primary evidence that led the later Hanafis (and Imam Abu Hanifa in his retraction) to require Quran recitation in Arabic?
A) A hadith stating that prayer is only valid in Arabic.
B) The Quran's description of itself as 'Arabic tongue' in multiple verses.
C) The consensus of the Sahaba.
D) The difficulty of understanding translation.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school's operative position, after Imam Abu Hanifa's retraction, requires Quran recitation in Arabic during prayer, as the Quran's own description of itself as 'Arabic tongue' overrides the earlier permissive view.
